When the questioner invalidates the definition by lack of comprehensiveness or lack of exclusivity, then the definer—meaning the one who committed to its correctness whether it originated from him or not—may deny the major premise (i.e., the universality). Denial is asking for evidence for the premise of the argument. He only prioritized denial of the universality of the major over denial of the minor itself because the major is the mainstay of the argument, such that they said the conclusion is potentially contained in it. Hence the major is treated in dialectics with the treatment of approximation, and because its denial is like a preface for clarifying the divisions of definition and as a notification that it is appropriate for the responder to wait until the questioner completes his argument and finishes, then deny (with support), i.e., the state of the definer being supported by saying that the definition is verbal. And this is only correct if the definition is absolutely more specific or in some respect, or absolutely more general or in some respect. But if the definition is divergent, then supporting by it is not correct; so this support is a more specific support. Know that the definer may deny the major premise without support, and that is the bare denial, and it is also valid. So the restriction is either based on illustration or on the more common. And every definition of such status is invalid: this is the exposition of the first (defect). (Or) that this definition is a definition not exclusive of its others. And the better is 'and' instead of 'or'. He said in the gloss: 'not comprehensive of the individuals of the definiendum' is a negation of universal affirmation, and similarly 'not exclusive of its others' ends. Meaning they are equivalent to negation of universal affirmation because they are with negated predicate, since they are minor premises for the first figure. And 'every definition of this status is invalid' is the exposition of the second. As for the exposition of the refutation by entailment of absurdity, it will come if Allah wills. And as for the exposition of the remaining four forms, it is needless of explanation. And know that when the questioner invalidates the definition by lack of comprehensiveness or lack of exclusivity, then the definer may deny the universality of the major premise. He presented the explanation of denial of the major because the knowledge of its divisions leads him to it, unlike denial of the minor, so he delayed it and placed a chapter for it because it is lengthy. (Denying) with support that this definition is verbal, by which the meaning of the word defined is interpreted. Yes, the bare denial is valid. It was said: if the denied (premise) is not self-evident or inductive, but many dialecticians often deny a thing without support, so it was not paid attention to in the exposition of the dialectic and sufficed with stating its being valid at the end of this chapter. Comprehensive of its conditions, and it is permissible to present this argument from the straightforward conditional and others, but the former (is preferred). On page 16: 'Lafdh' (Word). So it is not objected that this explanation is deficient. And the correctness of this denial and its support is that definitions are two types: verbal definition, intended to know the meaning of the word, and it is of the type of assents according to al-Muhaqqiq al-Jurjani, and of the conceptual requirement according to al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani, and hadd and rasm are not conceived in it. And real definition. Know that 'real' is used in the context of definition in three meanings. First: what conveys a form not yet attained, whether by mere essentials or not, and whether after knowledge of the existence of the definiendum or not; in this sense it is opposed to verbal and demonstrative. Second: what conveys a form not yet attained by mere essentials, whether after knowledge of existence or not; in this sense it is opposed to verbal, demonstrative, and descriptive (rasmi). Third: what conveys a form not yet attained, whether by mere essentials or not, but after knowledge of existence; in this sense it is opposed to instinctive, demonstrative, and nominal. It is not said that this division is not comprehensive of its divisions due to the exclusion of demonstrative definition. For we say: this definition is of the type of dividing a part into its well-known parts. And its summary is specification of the divided concept to other than demonstrative. And it can be answered that we do not concede its exclusion from the division; why may it not enter into verbal definition by naming the word 'verbal' and its definition and not giving an example for it? Either based on illustration or sufficing with the more common. So let it be considered. And the first type is the determination of the meaning of the word defined. And what is in it of facility is not hidden. And the intended is that by which its meaning is determined by another word, which is the definition, whether it is synonymous or a compound expressing its meaning or not, clear in its indication of that meaning intended to be determined, with clarity appropriate to the listener. Like defining 'al-ghadanfar' by 'al-asad', which is a definition by synonym, and 'al-asad' is clear in its indication of the predatory animal relative to the listener, unlike 'al-ghadanfar' because it is a rare language for the predatory animal. And its right is to be by a single word, whether synonymous or more general or more specific; if the single word is not found, mention the compound intended for determination. He only restricted clarity relative to the listener because if it were not clear relative to him, it would not be a verbal definition. For example, if the listener knows the meaning of 'al-qisas' (retaliation) but does not know the meaning of 'al-qawd' (retaliation), one says 'al-qawd is al-qisas', meaning that 'al-qisas' is set for the meaning of 'al-qawd', and they are two synonymous words. If the situation were reversed, the matter would be reversed, so one says 'al-qisas is al-qawd'. Hence they said that verbal definition admits mutual interchange. And it (i.e., the determination of the meaning of a word) is the way of the people of language. And the intended by 'language' is all Arabic sciences, not the mere language. And it is permissible with the more general and the more specific, meaning that the original is with the synonymous and the equivalent compound, but it is permissible with the more general and the more specific. And the correctness of this denial is that definitions are two types: verbal, intended to know the meaning of the word, and it is of the type of assents, and hadd nor rasm are conceived in it; and real, intended to acquire a stored form or not yet attained in the mind, so the nominal definition is called a real definition in this sense, so the restriction of the division is not contradicted. And the first type is the determination of the meaning of the word for the convention by another word, whether it is synonymous with the definiendum or a compound expressing its meaning or not, clear in its indication of that meaning intended to be determined relative to the listener (the addressee). And better is to say: a word by which the meaning of another word is determined, etc. He said in the gloss: 'And this is like defining al-ghadanfar by al-asad, and this is a definition by synonym, and al-asad is clear in indication of the predatory animal relative to the listener, unlike al-ghadanfar because it is a rare language for the predatory animal.' End. And like explaining 'existence' as 'what is either an agent or a patient'. And it (i.e., determination of the meaning of a word) is the way of the people of language and Arab scholars. And this determination is permissible with the absolutely general, so it is not exclusive, and with the absolutely specific, so it is not comprehensive, so it is true.

Commentary
This passage is a combination of dialectical strategy and definitional classification. It begins by describing the dialectical situation: when a questioner (sā'il) invalidates a definition by pointing out that it is either not comprehensive or not exclusive, the definer (the one who holds the definition) has the option to deny the major premise (man' al-kubrā) of the syllogism that was used to refute him. Denial (man') means demanding proof for the premise. The author notes that it is better to deny the universality of the major premise before denying the minor premise, because the major is the core of the argument, and its denial serves as a prelude to discussing the types of definitions. The definer can deny with support (mustanidan) by claiming that the definition is verbal (lafzī), i.e., it only explains the meaning of a word, not the essence. This is valid only if the definition is more specific or more general in an absolute sense or in some respect; if it is divergent (mubāyin), this support does not work. The definer may also simply deny without support (al-man' al-mujarrad), which is also considered valid, though the more common practice is to provide support. The author then reiterates the two defects with their explanations. Then, on page 16, the text turns to the classification of definitions. The support for the denial is that definitions are of two types: verbal (lafzī) and real (haqīqī). Verbal definition aims to clarify the meaning of a word, and it belongs to the realm of assents (tasdiqāt) according to al-Jurjānī, or to tasawwurī demands according to al-Taftāzānī. It does not involve true definition (hadd) or description (rasm). Real definition is then elaborated with three meanings: (1) conveying a form not yet attained, whether by essential predicates or not, regardless of prior knowledge of existence—opposed to verbal and demonstrative; (2) conveying a form not yet attained by essential predicates only—opposed to verbal, demonstrative, and descriptive; (3) conveying a form not yet attained, but only after knowledge of existence—opposed to instinctive, demonstrative, and nominal. The author addresses a potential objection that this division excludes demonstrative definition, defending it by saying that it is a division of a part into its well-known parts, i.e., it limits the divided concept to exclude demonstrative. Alternatively, one could argue that demonstrative definition might be included in verbal definition by a broader interpretation. Then the author explains the first type (verbal definition) in detail: it is the determination of the meaning of a word by another word that is clear to the listener, whether synonymous, or compound, or more general or more specific. The key is clarity to the listener; if the listener understands 'asad' better than 'ghadanfar', then defining the latter with the former is valid. This can be reversed, so verbal definitions are interchangeable. This method is the way of lexicographers. It is permissible to use a more general or more specific term, even though the ideal is a synonym or equivalent compound. The passage concludes by reiterating the correctness of the denial based on the division of definitions into verbal and real.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the first meaning of real definition is opposed to which types?
A) Verbal and demonstrative
B) Verbal, demonstrative, and descriptive
C) Instinctive, demonstrative, and nominal
D) Verbal and descriptive

Key Takeaway
Real definition can be understood in three ways; the simplest is any definition that conveys a new form, regardless of how.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following definitions as verbal or real (and if real, which meaning): (a) 'Man is a rational animal' (real, meaning 2); (b) 'Wine is derived from grapes' (verbal); (c) 'Riba is increase in a loan' (verbal).

Dialectic -- Jadal
advanced
5 min
When to Deny the Major Premise in Definitions
Author explains when and how to deny the major premise in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when to deny the major premise in a definition-based dialectical exchange based on the definition's scope.

Core Idea
The scope of a definition determines whether you can deny the major premise or must use another strategy.

In dialectics, definitions are often challenged by critics. The author of a definition can deny the major premise, but this move is not always valid. It depends on whether the definition is broader, narrower, or overlapping in scope. This lesson unpacks the conditions for denying the major premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope type of the definition
Determine whether the definition is absolutely broader (a'am mutlaq), absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq), or overlapping from one aspect (min wajh) relative to the definiendum.
2
Determine the intended purpose based on scope
For broader definitions, the intended purpose is to distinguish the definiendum from some things (not all). For narrower definitions, it is to explain well-known individuals. For overlapping, it may combine both.
3
Check the critic's objection against the intended purpose
If the critic's objection goes beyond the intended purpose (e.g., demanding the broader definition to cover all individuals), then the definer may deny the major premise by citing the intended purpose.
4
Apply the rule: Deny major premise only if justified
The denial must be supported by a specific basis (sanad a’s) that aligns with the intended purpose. Otherwise, the denial is invalid.
5
Recognize when to deny the minor premise instead
If the definition contradicts convention (mukhalif li al-‘urf), the definer cannot deny the major premise but must deny the minor premise by redefining the definiendum or the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منع الكبرى أيضا منعا مستندا بسند أخص بأن المراد من التعريف تميز المعرف عن بعض الأشياء إذا كان التعريف أعم مطلقا أو من وجه أو بيان الأفراد المشهورة إذا كان التعريف أخص مطلقا أو من وجه. تقطن فتح الله المشكلات عليك. محتمل أن يكون وجه الأمر بالتقطن أن الجواب لا يطابق السؤال لأنه مبنى على مذهب المتأخرين والجواب على مذهب المتقدمين فلا مطابقة بينهما. ويمكن دفعه بأن السؤال أيضا مبنى على مذهب المتقدمين بتوهم اشتراط المساواة عندهم أيضا، وبأن الجواب تحقيقي لا جدلي. ويحتمل أن يكون وجه التفطن أن صاحب التعريف إنما يمنع الكبرى إذا كان تصوير النقض ما قرره. وأما إذا قرر بأن هذا التعريف مباين للعرف فيكون فاسدا فلا مجال لمنع الكبرى بل يمنع الصغرى مستندا بتحرير المعرف أو التعريف. ويحتمل أن يكون وجهه أن دليل النقض كما يقبل المنع كذلك يقبل النقض والمعارضة فالاكتفاء مبنى على التمثيل أو على الأغلب المشهورة.
Denial of the major premise also, a denial supported by a more specific basis: that the intended meaning of the definition is to distinguish the definiendum from some things if the definition is absolutely broader or from one aspect, or to explain the well-known individuals if the definition is absolutely more specific or from one aspect. 'Taqattan Fath Allah al-mushkilat 'alayka.' It is possible that the reason for the command to pay attention is that the answer does not correspond to the question because it is based on the doctrine of the later scholars while the answer is based on the doctrine of the earlier scholars, so there is no correspondence between them. It can be refuted that the question itself is based on the doctrine of the earlier scholars by assuming that they also require equivalence, and that the answer is investigative, not dialectical. It is also possible that the reason for paying attention is that the author of the definition only denies the major premise if the formulation of the criticism is as he determined. But if he determined that this definition contradicts the convention, then it is invalid, and there is no room for denying the major premise; rather, he denies the minor premise, supported by redefining the definiendum or the definition. It is also possible that the reason is that the evidence of criticism accepts denial as well as criticism and counter-argument, so sufficing with denial is based on illustration or on the well-known majority.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage discusses the denial of the major premise (man' al-kubra) in the context of definitions. The author explains that such a denial must be supported by a more specific basis (sana a's). If the definition is absolutely broader (a'am mutlaq) or broader from one aspect (min wajh), the intended meaning of the definition is merely to distinguish the definiendum from some other things, not all. If the definition is absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq) or more specific from one aspect, the intended meaning is to explain the well-known individuals. The author then addresses a parenthetical note: 'taqattan Fath Allah al-mushkilat 'alayka' (which may be a marginal comment). Several explanations are given for why one should pay attention to this: it might be that the answer does not match the question because the latter is based on the doctrine of later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) while the answer is based on the doctrine of earlier scholars (mutaqaddimin). Alternatively, it might be that the question itself assumes a requirement of equivalence even among earlier scholars, so the answer is investigative (tahqiqi) not dialectical (jadali). Another explanation is that the definer only denies the major premise if the critic's formulation of the objection is as he (the definer) had determined; if the critic determines that the definition contradicts the convention, then the definition is clearly invalid, and the definer must instead deny the minor premise by redefining the definiendum or the definition. Finally, it might be that the evidence of criticism can itself be denied or countered, so sufficing with denial is based on illustration or common practice. This passage highlights the nuanced strategies in dialectical exchange regarding definitions.

أعم مطلقا

C) When the definition contradicts convention.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of denying the major premise in definition debates depends on the definition's scope and its intended purpose, and cannot be used when the definition contradicts convention.

Resolving Doctrinal Mismatches in Dialectical Definitions
Author explains reasons behind a command to pay attention in definition debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the possible reasons for a discrepancy between question and answer in a dialectical exchange about definitions.

Core Idea
A dialectical response must align with the doctrine assumed by the question or risk invalidity.

In the previous lesson, we saw the conditions for denying the major premise. The author then commands the reader to 'pay attention,' prompting several explanations. Why is this command necessary? Because the answer provided (about denying major premise) might not match the question's framework. This lesson explores the possible mismatches and how they are resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrinal basis of the question
Determine whether the question is framed according to the doctrine of earlier scholars (mutaqaddimin) or later scholars (muta'akhkhirin).
2
Identify the doctrinal basis of the answer
The answer denying the major premise may be based on earlier or later doctrine. Check which one the author of the definition is using.
3
Check for correspondence
If the question assumes later doctrine and the answer assumes earlier doctrine (or vice versa), there is a mismatch that needs resolution.
4
Consider possible reconciliations
One resolution is that the question itself also assumes earlier doctrine by implying a requirement of equivalence. Another is that the answer is investigative (tahqiqi) rather than dialectical (jadali), so doctrinal correspondence is not required.
5
Apply the same analysis to alternative scenarios
The command to pay attention may also refer to cases where the definer's denial strategy depends on the critic's formulation, requiring careful attention to the specific situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منع الكبرى أيضا منعا مستندا بسند أخص بأن المراد من التعريف تميز المعرف عن بعض الأشياء إذا كان التعريف أعم مطلقا أو من وجه أو بيان الأفراد المشهورة إذا كان التعريف أخص مطلقا أو من وجه. تقطن فتح الله المشكلات عليك. محتمل أن يكون وجه الأمر بالتقطن أن الجواب لا يطابق السؤال لأنه مبنى على مذهب المتأخرين والجواب على مذهب المتقدمين فلا مطابقة بينهما. ويمكن دفعه بأن السؤال أيضا مبنى على مذهب المتقدمين بتوهم اشتراط المساواة عندهم أيضا، وبأن الجواب تحقيقي لا جدلي. ويحتمل أن يكون وجه التفطن أن صاحب التعريف إنما يمنع الكبرى إذا كان تصوير النقض ما قرره. وأما إذا قرر بأن هذا التعريف مباين للعرف فيكون فاسدا فلا مجال لمنع الكبرى بل يمنع الصغرى مستندا بتحرير المعرف أو التعريف. ويحتمل أن يكون وجهه أن دليل النقض كما يقبل المنع كذلك يقبل النقض والمعارضة فالاكتفاء مبنى على التمثيل أو على الأغلب المشهورة.
Denial of the major premise also, a denial supported by a more specific basis: that the intended meaning of the definition is to distinguish the definiendum from some things if the definition is absolutely broader or from one aspect, or to explain the well-known individuals if the definition is absolutely more specific or from one aspect. 'Taqattan Fath Allah al-mushkilat 'alayka.' It is possible that the reason for the command to pay attention is that the answer does not correspond to the question because it is based on the doctrine of the later scholars while the answer is based on the doctrine of the earlier scholars, so there is no correspondence between them. It can be refuted that the question itself is based on the doctrine of the earlier scholars by assuming that they also require equivalence, and that the answer is investigative, not dialectical. It is also possible that the reason for paying attention is that the author of the definition only denies the major premise if the formulation of the criticism is as he determined. But if he determined that this definition contradicts the convention, then it is invalid, and there is no room for denying the major premise; rather, he denies the minor premise, supported by redefining the definiendum or the definition. It is also possible that the reason is that the evidence of criticism accepts denial as well as criticism and counter-argument, so sufficing with denial is based on illustration or on the well-known majority.

Commentary
This passage discusses the denial of the major premise (man' al-kubra) in the context of definitions. The author explains that such a denial must be supported by a more specific basis (sana a's). If the definition is absolutely broader (a'am mutlaq) or broader from one aspect (min wajh), the intended meaning of the definition is merely to distinguish the definiendum from some other things, not all. If the definition is absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq) or more specific from one aspect, the intended meaning is to explain the well-known individuals. The author then addresses a parenthetical note: 'taqattan Fath Allah al-mushkilat 'alayka' (which may be a marginal comment). Several explanations are given for why one should pay attention to this: it might be that the answer does not match the question because the latter is based on the doctrine of later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) while the answer is based on the doctrine of earlier scholars (mutaqaddimin). Alternatively, it might be that the question itself assumes a requirement of equivalence even among earlier scholars, so the answer is investigative (tahqiqi) not dialectical (jadali). Another explanation is that the definer only denies the major premise if the critic's formulation of the objection is as he (the definer) had determined; if the critic determines that the definition contradicts the convention, then the definition is clearly invalid, and the definer must instead deny the minor premise by redefining the definiendum or the definition. Finally, it might be that the evidence of criticism can itself be denied or countered, so sufficing with denial is based on illustration or common practice. This passage highlights the nuanced strategies in dialectical exchange regarding definitions.

The Third Mode of Refutation: Dawr and Tasalsul
Author explains the method of refuting a definition by showing it leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutual and sequential circularity, explain the conditions under which infinite regress is impossible in kalam vs. philosophy, and apply the methods of denying implication or impossibility in dialectical refutations.

Core Idea
Not every circularity or regress is impossible — the key is whether it occurs between definiens and definiendum or involves actual existence and arrangement.

This passage comes from a classical jadal text and teaches the third mode of refutation, where a definition is invalidated by showing it implies circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). It also explains how the defender can counter by denying the implication (man' al-istilzam) through refining the definition, or denying the impossibility (man' al-istihalah) by arguing the circularity or regress is of a permissible type. Understanding these moves is essential for sound dialectical practice in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refutation claim
The refuter (sa'il) asserts that the definition implies an impossibility, specifically circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). Locate this claim in the passage: the definition is said to be 'mustalzim' (implying) for dawr or tasalsul.
2
Determine the type of circularity or regress
Is the circularity mutual (dawr ma'i) or sequential (dawr taqaddumi)? Is the regress in actual existents or mental/non-existent things? The passage distinguishes these, noting that mutual circularity is permissible except between definiens and definiendum, and that philosophers allow regress under three conditions.
Use the examples: 'father' defined as 'one who has a son' (impermissible mutual circularity) vs. parts of a definition (permissible).
3
Assess the defender's response
The defender (sahib al-ta'rif) can either deny the implication (man' al-istilzam) by refining (tahrir) the definition, or deny the impossibility (man' al-istihalah) by arguing the type of dawr or tasalsul is not impossible. Which response is appropriate given the type?
4
Evaluate the logical formulations
The author presents two syllogistic structures (taswiran). In the first, the impossibility claim is part of the minor; in the second, it is the major of a preliminary syllogism. Trace how the two formulations lead to the same conclusion.
This step shows the compound nature of the argument (two syllogisms in the second formulation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في تقرير الإبطال بالثالث وهو النقض باستلزامه المحال وهو أن هذا التعريف مستلزم للدور سواء كان بين التعريف والمعرف أو بين جزء التعريف والمعرف أو بين أجزاء التعريف أو مستلزم للتسلسل مطلقا وهو ترتيب أمور غير متناهية وهو مبني على التمثيل فلا يرد عليه أنه قد يستلزم محالا آخر كلب الشيء عن نفسه واجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما وعدم الفيض على النقيض والترجيح بلا مرجح وغيره من المحالات. وهو محال وكل تعريف يستلزم المحال فهو فاسد ينتج أن هذا التعريف فاسد. اعلم أن ههنا تصويرين. أحدهما أن الصغرى مع قيدها وهو قوله وهو محال صغرى وما بعده كبرى وهو قياس واحد. وثانيهما أن قوله وهو محال كبرى للقياس الأول بأن يقال إن هذا التعريف مستلزم للدور أو التسلسل وكل دور أو تسلسل محال ينتج أن هذا التعريف مستلزم للمحال وتضم إلى هذه النتيجة الكبرى المذكورة هكذا وكل مستلزم للمحال فهو فاسد فهذا التعريف فاسد فعلى هذا يكون الدليل مركبا من دليلين: أحدهما غير متعارف والآخر متعارف ويطلق على المركبة من المقدمتين صغرى مسامحة لكونها قائمة مقامها. ولما تم الكلام على وظائف السائل أشار إلى وظائف صاحب التعريف بقوله ولا مجال لمنع الكبرى هنا بل يمنع الاستلزام وهو الصغرى في التقريرين مع قطع النظر عن القيد وسنده في الغالب تحرير التعريف أي بيان المراد من التعريف كلا أو بعضا ويجوز تغيير أجزاء التعريف كلا أو بعضا وتحرير المعرف. وأما تغيير المعرف فغير صحيح ومادة نقض التعريف وتحرير المذهب الذي بني عليه التعريف فظهر فائدة التقييد بقوله في الغالب أو يمنع الاستحالة سواء كان قيدا كما في التقرير الأول أو كبرى كما في التقرير الثاني أو يمنع الاستحالة والاستلزام معا لكن على تقدير التسليم بأن يقال لا نسلم أنه يستلزم الدور أو التسلسل وإن سلم أنه مستلزم لأحدهما فلا نسلم أنه دور محال أو تسلسل محال فظهر أن كلمة أو لمنع الخلو منعا مستندا بأن هذا الدور غير محال لأنه دور معي بين أجزاء التعريف أو أن هذا التسلسل غير محال لأنه تسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية أو في الأمور المعدة ولا يخفى أن هذا تصوير للمنع لا سنده كما لا يخفى على من تتبع مناظرات المؤلفين اللهم إلا أن يقال إنه سمي سندا مسامحة لكونه توطئة للسند كما أشرنا إليه. وبيان محالها أي تمييز القسم المحال من الدور والتسلسل عن عدم محالها أي عن القسم غير المحال لهما في علم الكلام. اعلم أن الدور إما معي أو تقدمي. أما الدور المعي فهو كون الشيء مع الآخر كالمتضايفين فان حصول كل منهما في المفكرة يستلزم حصول الآخر فيها معا بلا تقدم من أحدهما على الآخر وهو ليس بمحال إلا أن يقع بين المعرف والتعريف كتعريف الأب بمن له ابن. قال المحقق التفتازاني في شرح الشمسية: أحد المتضايفين لا يجوز أخذه في تعريف الآخر لأن الحد يجب أن يعقل قبل المحدود والمتضايفان يكون تعلقهما معا. وأما الدور التقدمي فهو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه بمرتبة أو بمراتب وهو إما مصرح أو مضمر. أما المصرح فكتعريف الكيفية بما يقع به للمشابهة واللامشابهة والمشابهة اتفاق في الكيفية. وأما المضمر فكما يقال الاثنان الزوج الأول ثم يقال الزوج الأول هو النقم بمتساويين ثم يقال المتساويان هما الشيئان اللذان لا يفضل أحدهما عن الآخر ثم يقال الشيئان الاثنان وأن التسلسل ترتيب أمور غير متناهية وهو إما في جانب العلل إن أخذ العلول أولاء واستفسر عن العلة أو في جانب المعلول إن أخذ بالعكس وكل منهما محال عند المتكلمين بل حكموا باستحالة مطلق الأمور غير المتناهية سواء كانت مرتبة أو لا وسواء كانت مجتمعة في الوجود أو لا. وأما الحكماء فاشترطوا في استحالته أمورا ثلاثة كون الأمور مجتمعة وموجودة ومرتبة لأن جريان برهان التطبيق والتضايف بل البرهان العرشي أيضا موقوف عليها فتبطله. فإذا انتفى أحد الأمور بأن لم تكن الأمور موجودة كما بين المعلومات أو كانت موجودة ولم تكن مجتمعة كما بين المعدات أو كانت مجمعة ولم تكن مرتبة كما بين النفوس الناطقة لم يكن محالا عندهم كما هو المشهور. ويكفيك هنا هذا الإجمال
Chapter: On establishing invalidation through the third [mode], which is the refutation by its implication of the impossible. That is, this definition implies circularity, whether between the definition and the definiendum, or between a part of the definition and the definiendum, or between parts of the definition; or it implies infinite regress absolutely—which is an arrangement of infinite things—and it is based on exemplification, so it is not objected that it might imply another impossibility like the inversion of a thing against itself, conjunction of two opposites, their disjunction, lack of emanation over the opposite, preference without a preferrer, and other impossibilities. And it is impossible, and every definition that implies the impossible is invalid, producing the result that this definition is invalid. Know that here there are two formulations. One [is] that the minor premise with its restriction—that is, his saying 'and it is impossible'—[is] the minor, and what follows it is the major, and it is a single syllogism. The second [is] that his saying 'and it is impossible' is the major for the first syllogism, such that it is said: This definition implies circularity or infinite regress, and every circularity or infinite regress is impossible, producing that this definition implies the impossible. Then you attach to this result the stated major as follows: And everything that implies the impossible is invalid. So this definition is invalid. According to this, the proof is composed of two proofs: one unconventional and the other conventional. And the compound of the two premises is called a minor loosely because it stands in its place. And when the discussion of the functions of the questioner was completed, he indicated the functions of the owner of the definition by his saying: 'There is no scope for denying the major here.' Rather, the implication is denied—which is the minor in both formulations, disregarding the restriction—and its basis is mostly the refinement of the definition, i.e., clarifying the intended meaning of the definition, wholly or partially. And it is permissible to change parts of the definition, wholly or partially, and to refine the definiendum. As for changing the definiendum, it is incorrect. The substance of refuting the definition and refining the doctrine upon which the definition is based appears as the benefit of the qualification 'mostly'. Or the impossibility is denied, whether it is a restriction as in the first formulation or a major as in the second formulation. Or both the impossibility and the implication are denied together, but under the condition of concession, such that it is said: We do not concede that it implies circularity or infinite regress, and if it is conceded that it implies one of them, we do not concede that it is an impossible circularity or impossible infinite regress. Thus it appears that the word 'or' is for preventing vacancy, a prevention based on the claim that this circularity is not impossible because it is a circularity of mutual dependence between parts of the definition, or that this infinite regress is not impossible because it is an infinite regress in mental matters or in non-existent things. And it is not hidden that this is a depiction of the prevention, not its basis, as is not hidden for one who follows the debates of authors, unless it is said that it is called a basis loosely because it is a prelude to the basis, as we have indicated. And explaining their impossibility [means] distinguishing the impossible part of circularity and infinite regress from their non-impossibility, i.e., from the part that is not impossible for them, in the science of kalam. Know that circularity is either mutual [dawr ma'i] or sequential [dawr taqaddumi]. As for mutual circularity, it is a thing being with another like correlatives. For the occurrence of each of them in the mind implies the occurrence of the other in it simultaneously, without priority of one over the other, and it is not impossible unless it occurs between the definiens and the definiendum, like defining 'father' as 'one who has a son'. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani said in Sharh al-Shamsiyya: 'It is not permissible to take one of two correlatives in the definition of the other, because the definition must be understood before the definiendum, and the two correlatives have their relation simultaneously.' As for sequential circularity, it is a thing depending upon that upon which it depends by a degree or degrees, and it is either explicit or implicit. As for the explicit, like defining quality as 'that by which similarity and dissimilarity occur'—and similarity is agreement in quality. As for the implicit, like when it is said: Two is the first even number; then it is said: The first even number is that which is divided into two equal halves; then it is said: The two equal halves are two things that do not exceed one another; then it is said: The two things are two. And infinite regress is an arrangement of infinite things, and it is either on the side of causes if the effects are taken first and the cause is sought, or on the side of effects if the opposite is taken. And each of them is impossible according to the mutakallimun; rather, they ruled the impossibility of absolute infinite things, whether arranged or not, and whether coexistent in existence or not. As for the philosophers, they stipulated three conditions for its impossibility: that the things be coexistent, existent, and arranged, because the application of the proof of application and correlation—indeed, the 'arshite proof—depends on them, so they invalidate it. So if one of these conditions is absent—such as if the things are not existent, like among the known, or they are existent but not coexistent, like among non-existent things, or they are coexistent but not arranged, like among rational souls—it is not impossible according to them, as is well-known. And this summary suffices you here.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the third mode of refutation (al-ibtal bi al-thalith), which is the refutation of a definition by showing that it leads to an impossibility—specifically, circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author first states that the refutation consists in showing that the definition implies circularity (whether between the definiens and definiendum, or between parts of the definition) or implies infinite regress. He notes that this method is based on exemplification, so it is not open to the objection that it might imply another impossibility (like the inversion of a thing against itself, etc.). The core argument is: if a definition implies an impossibility, then the definition is invalid. The author then presents two logical formulations (taswiran) of this argument. In the first, the minor premise includes the claim that the implication is impossible; in the second, the impossibility claim is the major premise. He explains that in the second formulation, the proof is composed of two syllogisms. After discussing the questioner's duties, he turns to the defender (sahib al-ta'rif) and explains that the defender has two main options: either deny the implication (man' al-istilzam) or deny the impossibility (man' al-istihalah). The basis for denying the implication is usually a refinement of the definition (tahrir al-ta'rif), either clarifying the intended meaning or modifying parts of the definition. The author also notes that changing the definiendum itself is not allowed. For denying the impossibility, the defender can argue that the circularity is not impossible because it is 'mutual circularity' (dawr ma'i), which is permissible when it occurs between parts of the same definition, or that the infinite regress is not impossible because it occurs in mental or non-existent things. The author then provides a deeper explanation of the types of circularity and infinite regress, drawing on kalam and philosophy. In kalam, any infinite regress is impossible, while philosophers require three conditions (coexistence, existence, and arrangement) for impossibility. The author ends by stating that this summary is sufficient for now. Throughout, the commentary follows the author's own order, clarifying each point as it appears.

Key Terms
الإبطال بالثالث (al-ibtal bi al-thalith): The third mode of refutation, which is refuting a definition by showing it implies an impossibility such as circularity or infinite regress. [Note: This is a standard jadal method; no significant shift across the tradition.]
الدور (al-dawr): Circularity in definition or argument, comprising both mutual (ma'i) and sequential (taqaddumi) types. Only sequential is always impossible; mutual can be permissible except between definiens and definiendum. [Note: In later works, dawr is often unconditionally rejected; here it is nuanced with exceptions.]
التسلسل (al-tasalsul): An infinite regress of causes or effects. Fully impossible according to mutakallimun, but philosophers allow it under certain conditions. [Note: Philosophers' conditional acceptance differs from the strict rejection in kalam.]
منع الاستلزام (man' al-istilzam): Denying the implication of the impossible by refining or modifying the definition to show no actual circularity or regress. [Note: Standard jadal tactic; stable term.]
منع الاستحالة (man' al-istihalah): Denying that the implied circularity or regress is impossible, often by arguing it is a permissible type (e.g., mutual circularity in parts of definition). [Note: Same concept appears in later jadal texts.]
تحرير التعريف (tahrir al-ta'rif): Clarifying or modifying the definition to remove the appearance of circularity or regress. [Note: Common technique in dialectic; stable meaning.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an acceptable (non-impossible) type of circularity?
A) Circularity between the definiens and the definiendum, like defining 'father' as 'one who has a son'.
B) Sequential circularity (dawr taqaddumi) with multiple steps.
C) Mutual circularity (dawr ma'i) between parts of the same definition.
D) Circularity in a causal chain.

Key Takeaway
The third mode of refutation is a powerful tool in jadal, but its success depends on correctly identifying the type of circularity or regress and knowing when it is genuinely impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: A jurist defines 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) as 'extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared 'illah'. The questioner objects that this definition implies circularity because 'illah is defined by its role in qiyas. Identify the type of circularity (if any) and suggest how the defender could respond using tahrir al-ta'rif or man' al-istihalah.
?
Task 2: In a kalam debate, a mutakallim defines 'knowledge' as 'the attribute that discloses the known'. A critic claims this leads to infinite regress because the 'known' must be known first. Determine whether this regress is of the impossible type according to the mutakallimun or philosophers, and explain why.

Roles in Definition Disputes: The Objector as Prover, the Responder as Preventer
Author clarifies the roles of objector and responder in definition disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the roles of nāqiḍ and mujīb in a definition dispute and explain the required forms of their arguments (mustadill and māni').

Core Idea
Objecting requires proof; defending requires preventing a premise.

In debates over definitions—common in fiqh, kalam, and usul—it is not enough to simply reject a definition. The Waldiyya commentary clarifies that the objector (nāqiḍ) must provide evidence (istidlāl) for the invalidity, and the responder (mujīb) must prevent (man') one or more premises of that evidence. This lesson unpacks these roles and their logical foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles
The passage distinguishes two participants: the objector (nāqiḍ al-ta'rīf) who challenges the definition, and the responder (mujīb) who defends it. Recognize who is the mustadill (prover) and who is the māni' (preventer).
The objector is also called the mu'tarid.
2
Understand the objector's requirement
The objector must not merely reject the definition; he must claim it is invalid and provide a proof (istidlāl) for that claim. The proof must be based on known flaws: lack of comprehensiveness, lack of exclusiveness, entailing impossibility, or not being clearer than the definiendum.
This distinguishes a valid objection from a bare assertion.
3
Understand the responder's requirement
The responder (mujīb) must answer by preventing (man') one or more premises of the objector's proof. He does not need to reject the entire proof; he can target a specific premise.
The responder is thus a māni' (preventer), not a counter-prover.
4
Recognize the dialectical structure
The passage explains that correctness and correspondence of a definition are implicit claims that admit prevention. The objector's role is to refute (naqd) with proof; the responder's role is to prevent (man'). This is the customary method in Islamic dialectic.
This structured exchange prevents endless debate and keeps the discussion focused on logical grounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the dialectical rules concerning definitional objections. It begins by stating a well-known principle among students: the person who objects to a definition (nāqiḍ al-ta'rīf) is considered a proponent of a proof (mustadill), and the person who responds to the objection (mujīb) is considered a preventer (māni'). The author critiques the phrasing of this principle, noting that 'proof' is already inherent in the concept of refutation. He then clarifies: the objector's objection must take the form of a claim that the definition is invalid, supported by evidence. This evidence can be based on any of the known flaws: lack of comprehensiveness, lack of exclusiveness, implying an impossibility, or not being clearer than the definiendum. The responder, in turn, must answer by preventing (man') one or more premises of that evidence. The passage then explains that the correctness, goodness, and correspondence of a definition are implicit claims that can be contested, and the invalidation of a definition is itself a claim that requires proof and is subject to refutation. In practice, the custom is limited: the objector raises a refutation (naqd), and the author of the definition responds by preventing (man') a premise. This is a concise summary of the dialectical method for handling definitions in the Islamic logical tradition, emphasizing that both parties must follow structured argumentation rather than making bald assertions.

A) He may simply state his objection without proof.
B) He must provide a proof (istidlāl) for the claim of invalidity.
C) He must accept the definition and then propose a better one.
D) He only objects if the definition is verbal (lafẓī).

Key Takeaway
In definition dialectics, the objector must prove invalidity with evidence, and the responder must prevent a premise of that proof.

The Specific Meaning of 'Man'' in the Waldiyya
Author defines 'man'' as request for proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the meaning of 'man'' in the Waldiyya and explain why it is considered a metaphorical usage.

Core Idea
In this treatise, 'man'' means 'request for proof', not any objection.

A common confusion in Islamic dialectic is the meaning of 'man'' (objection). While in some works it covers all refutations, the author of the Waldiyya restricts its meaning to a specific request for evidence. This lesson unpacks that definition and its metaphorical nature.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the passage where the author states that 'man'' in this treatise means 'طلب الدليل' (request for proof) upon the premise of the proof.
The author explicitly says 'فهو بمعنى طلب الدليل'.
2
Identify the qualification
Note the qualifying phrase 'على مقدمة الدليل' (upon the premise of the proof). This restricts man' to a request focused on a specific premise of the opponent's proof.
This excludes requests for proof on the overall claim or other matters.
3
Understand metaphorical usage
The author explains that in the custom of dialecticians, man' literally means request for proof on the premise. Here it is used metaphorically to also cover requests on other parts (like the claim or transmission).
Metaphorical because the real meaning is more specific.
4
Compare with other books
Contrast with the usage in other books where man' can mean any refutation (daf') including by reasoning or invalidation.
This highlights the unique, focused meaning in the Waldiyya.

Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage clarifies the technical meaning of 'man'' (objection) as used in this treatise. The author distinguishes between man' and other terms like naqd and mu'araḍa. He states that in this treatise, wherever the word 'man'' appears, it specifically means 'request for proof' (talab al-dalil) upon the premise of the proof. This is a metaphorical usage, as the real meaning of man' in dialectical custom is more specific. However, the author notes that in some other books, man' is used to mean any form of refutation (daf'), whether by requesting proof or by reasoning. He then classifies different types of refutations: naqd tafsili (detailed refutation), munaqada, naqd ijmalī (general refutation), and mu'araḍa. The passage ends with a summary that man' in this treatise is always metaphorical, meaning request for proof, and that this usage covers objections in various chapters of the book.

Types of Objections: Man', Naqd, and Mu'araḍa
Author distinguishes types of objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man', naqḍ tafṣīlī, naqḍ ijmālī, and mu'āraḍa as types of objections in dialectic.

Core Idea
Not all objections are 'man''; some are detailed refutations, others are counterarguments.

The passage not only clarifies man' but also introduces other key terms: naqḍ tafṣīlī (detailed refutation), naqḍ ijmālī (general refutation), and mu'āraḍa (counterargument). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for proper jadal practice.

Guided Path
1
Locate the terms in the passage
Find where the terms 'نقض تفصيلي', 'نقض إجمالي', and 'معارضة' appear. The passage says 'ويسمى نقضا تفصيليا' for man', and later distinguishes naqḍ ijmālī and mu'āraḍa.
The Arabic: 'ويسمى طلب الدليل مطلقا نقضا تفصيليا' and then 'الأول نقض إجمالي والثاني معارضة'.
2
Understand the basis of distinction
The distinction is based on the nature of the refutation: man'/naqḍ tafṣīlī involves specifying the faulty premise and demanding proof; naqḍ ijmālī refutes generally (real or specious); mu'āraḍa counters with an opposing proof (by inversion, example, etc.).
The passage says naqḍ ijmālī is 'تحقيقيا أو شبهيا' and mu'āraḍa is 'تحقيقية أو تقديرية'.
3
Recognize man' as a subset
Man' is only the request for proof, while naqḍ and mu'āraḍa involve reasoning. The author notes that man' is sometimes called naqḍ tafṣīlī because the questioner specifies the location.
Man' is a specific type of objection; not all objections are man'.
4
Apply the taxonomy
Given a sample objection, decide which category it falls into: is it a request for proof (man'), a general refutation (naqḍ ijmālī), or a counterargument (mu'āraḍa)?
Use the definitions: man' = demands proof; naqḍ ijmālī = refutes without specifying a premise; mu'āraḍa = presents opposing proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أعلم أن المنع الذى هو الاعتراض احترز به عن المنع الذى فى عدم المنع في التعريف وعن المنع الذى فى منع التقسيم في التقسيم أينما وقع في هذه الرسالة سواء كان فى باب التعريف أو فى باب القسم أو في باب على عدم جريان المناظرة في التنبيهات وطلب الدليل أعم سواء كان على مقدمة الدليل أو على المدعى أو النقل وهذا التعمم مجاز في استعمال لفظ المنع إذ لفظ المنع في عرفهم طلب الدليل على مقدمة الدليل ، وسيأتي أوهم أن المنع المجرد ليس صحيح موجه في مقابلته
Then know that the 'man'' which is the objection (i'tirad) – he guarded by it against the 'man'' that is in 'adam al-man' (absence of prevention) in definition and against the 'man'' that is in prevent division in division. Wherever it occurs in this treatise, whether in the chapter on definition, or division, or tasdiq, or the conclusion, it – i.e., the word 'man'' – is associated with the meaning of 'request for proof' (talab al-dalil), i.e., in the sense of requesting the proof, and what is meant by 'proof' (al-dalil) is the clarifying proof, so it includes reminders (tanbihat) or it is of the type of omission of the conjunct, or of the type of sufficing with the original over the branch, or based on the non-occurrence of discussion in reminders. And requesting proof is general, whether it is upon the premise of the proof or upon the claim or transmission. And this generalization is metaphorical in the usage of the word 'man'' because the word 'man'' in their custom is the request for proof upon the premise of the proof. And the detail of this will come, God willing. And (it is called) the request for proof absolutely (naqd tafsili) because of the specification by the questioner and his determination of the location of the [fault?]. And it is also called (munaqada) and likewise what is derived from one of these words. And all of these are metaphorical words because their real meanings are the request for proof upon the premise of the proof. And (the mentioned word 'man') may be used in some books, meaning in some books of adab (آدانية?), meaning refutation (daf'), i.e., rebutting the proof or the claim or the definition or the division or the expression. And he indicated it by his saying: 'absolutely, whether the refutation is by requesting proof – which is munaqada absolutely, whether real or metaphorical, and whether with evidence or not – or by refutation through reasoning (ibṭāl bi-l-istidlāl).' And that includes the general naqd (naqd ijmalī), real or specious, and mu'araḍa (counterargument), real or hypothetical, whether in the claim or the premise, whether by inversion (qalb) or by example (mithl) or by another. Because it is either by invalidating the claim, proof, definition, division, or expression and reasoning upon it or not. The first is naqd ijmalī, the second is mu'araḍa. And since in requesting proof there is a kind of generality, some of the previous objections are man' and not naqḍ, he answered by saying: 'Then know that the word man' which is the objection means...' Wherever it occurs in this treatise, it is metaphorical, meaning request for proof (i.e., clarification) so it includes relative [requests?]. He said in the hashya: whether upon the premise of the proof or upon the claim, and this generalization is metaphorical in the usage of the word man' because the word man' in their custom is placed for requesting proof upon the premise of the proof. And its detail will come, end. Yes, it may be said that the word man' is also placed in their custom for proof in contrast to proof, whether by way of demand or refutation, so it includes naqḍ, munaqada, and mu'araḍa. And that is also metaphorical in that usage. And it is called detailed refutation because of the specification by the questioner and his determination of the faulty premise. And munaqada and mumani'a (?) are also metaphorical, because all these words are placed in their custom for requesting proof upon the premise of the proof. And the word man' may be used in some books metaphorically as refutation (daf'), absolutely, whether that refutation is by requesting proof or by refutation or by reasoning, so it includes all types of refutations. And the stronger view is that man' in contrast to naqḍ of definition with evidence always suggests that mere man' is not validly directed against it.

Commentary
This passage clarifies the technical meaning of 'man'' (objection) as used in this treatise. The author distinguishes between man' and other terms like naqd and mu'araḍa. He states that in this treatise, wherever the word 'man'' appears, it specifically means 'request for proof' (talab al-dalil) upon the premise of the proof. This is a metaphorical usage, as the real meaning of man' in dialectical custom is more specific. However, the author notes that in some other books, man' is used to mean any form of refutation (daf'), whether by requesting proof or by reasoning. He then classifies different types of refutations: naqd tafsili (detailed refutation), munaqada, naqd ijmalī (general refutation), and mu'araḍa. The passage ends with a summary that man' in this treatise is always metaphorical, meaning request for proof, and that this usage covers objections in various chapters of the book.

Bare Objection vs. Objection with Support
Author distinguishes bare objection from objection with support and defines sanad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a bare objection and an objection with support in a dialectical exchange and explain the role of sanad in strengthening objections.

Core Idea
Bare objection is valid, but a supported objection carries greater dialectical weight.

In Islamic dialectics (jadal), not all objections are equal. A simple denial of the opponent's claim is permissible, but a stronger strategy is to provide a reason (sanad) for the denial. This lesson clarifies the distinction between these two types of objection and the terminology used in the Waldiyya tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of objection
The passage mentions 'bare objection' as simply stating 'I do not accept' without further reasoning. 'Objection with support' adds a reason or evidence. Note the difference.
2
Understand the evaluation
The author says both are valid, but the objection with support is stronger. Reflect on why providing a reason strengthens the dialectical position.
In debate, a supported objection is harder to dismiss.
3
Define sanad in this context
The passage defines sanad as 'that which is mentioned to strengthen the objection'. Note that this is different from the hadith chain usage.
Sanad here means logical support, not a chain of narrators.
4
Apply the distinction
Practice recognizing whether a given objection is bare or supported, and consider how to add a sanad to a bare objection.
This skill is essential in real dialectical exchanges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن طلب الدليل قد يخلو عن ذكر السند كأن يقال لا نسلم ما ذكرته أو هو ممنوع ولا يزاد على هذا القدر ويسمى هذا المنع في عرفهم منعا مجردا. وقد يذكر معه سند وسيجيء تفصيل السند. والمنع المجرد صحيح لكن المنع مع السند أقوى منه. والسند في عرفهم ما يذكر لتقوية المنع.
Then, indeed, requesting proof may be devoid of mentioning a support, such as saying 'We do not accept what you mentioned' or 'It is rejected', and no more is added. This objection is called in their terminology 'bare objection'. And sometimes a support is mentioned with it, and the detail of support will come. The bare objection is valid, but the objection with support is stronger than it. And 'support' in their terminology is that which is mentioned to strengthen the objection.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage classifies objections (man') in dialectical debate into two types: bare objection (man' mujarrad) and objection with support (man' ma'a al-sanad). A bare objection is simply stating 'We do not accept' or 'That is rejected' without further justification. This is considered valid in the tradition. However, an objection that comes with a support (sanad) is stronger. The term 'sanad' (support) is defined as anything that is brought to strengthen the objection. The author notes that a detailed discussion of the types of sanad will come later in the treatise. The distinction emphasizes that while a bare objection is permissible, providing a rationale makes the objection more forceful in dialectical exchange.

Key Terms
المنع المجرد
السند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Waldiyya, what is a 'sanad' in the context of objection?
A) A chain of narrators
B) A premise that is attacked
C) Something mentioned to strengthen the objection
D) The conclusion of the argument

Key Takeaway
A bare objection is valid in dialectical debate, but an objection supported by a reason (sanad) is stronger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How could the objector turn it into a supported objection? Provide a sanad based on an Islamic theological premise.

The Bare Objection in Dialectic
Author explains the bare objection and defines sanad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a bare objection in a dialectical exchange and explain its validity relative to a supported objection.

Core Idea
A bare objection is valid but weaker than one with supporting evidence.

In formal Islamic dialectic (jadal), not every objection needs to be accompanied by a reason. The bare objection—simply denying the opponent's claim—is accepted as a legitimate move. However, its strength is limited. This lesson explains why.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the bare objection formula
The author gives examples: 'We do not accept what you mentioned' or 'It is denied, requiring clarification.' These are bare because they lack any supporting argument.
In Arabic: لا نسلم ما ذكرته or هو ممنوع مطلوب البيان.
2
Understand the permissibility of bare objection
The author explicitly says the bare objection (al-manع al-mujarrad) is valid. This means it is an acceptable move in dialectic, even without a supporting reason.
The text: 'والمنع المجرد صحيح'.
3
Compare bare and supported objections
The author states that the objection with support (sanad) is stronger. This implies that while a bare objection is valid, it is not as persuasive or effective as one with a sanad.
The text: 'لكن المنع مع السند أقوى منه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن طلب الدليل قد يخلو عن ذكر السند كأن يقال لا نسلم ما ذكرته أو منعه أو كأن يقال هو ممنوع مطلوب البيان ولا يزاد على هذا القدر ويسمى هذا المنع في عرفهم منها مجردا. والمنع المجرد صحيح. لكن المنع مع السند أقوى منه. والسند في عرفهم ما يذكر لتقوية المنع.
Then know that requesting evidence may be devoid of mentioning the support (sanad), such as saying 'We do not accept what you mentioned' or its objection, or such as saying 'It [i.e., what you mentioned] is denied, requiring clarification,' and no more is added beyond this amount. This objection is called in their terminology 'bare' (mujarrad). The bare objection is valid. But the objection with support is stronger than it. And 'support' in their terminology is what is mentioned to strengthen the objection.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage belongs to the field of dialectic (jadal). It explains the concept of 'objection' (manع) in debate. The author first states that a request for evidence may be made without citing any 'sanad' (support), meaning the objector simply says 'We do not accept' or 'This is denied' without providing a reason. Such a bare objection is valid and accepted in the discipline. However, if the objector accompanies his objection with a supporting reason, that objection is stronger. The author then defines 'sanad' as that which is mentioned to strengthen the objection. In a fiqh debate, for example, if one scholar says 'This contract is invalid', the opponent may simply say 'لا نسلم' (we do not accept); this is a bare objection and is permissible, but it is more effective to add a supporting argument like 'because its condition is missing'.

Key Terms
منع
سند
مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid and not accepted.
B) It is valid but weaker than an objection with support.
C) It is stronger than an objection with support.
D) It is only valid if the opponent allows it.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic dialectic, a bare objection—simply denying the opponent's claim—is valid but less powerful than one accompanied by a supporting reason (sanad).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Definition and Function of Sanad in Dialectic
Author defines sanad and its function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sanad in the context of jadal and explain how it strengthens an objection.

Core Idea
Sanad is the supporting reason that turns a weak objection into a strong one.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a bare objection is valid but weak. The strength comes from adding a sanad—a supporting argument. This lesson examines the definition and role of sanad in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of sanad
The author defines sanad as 'what is mentioned to strengthen the objection' (ما يذكر لتقوية المنع).
This definition is a rasm (description) identifying the purpose of sanad.
2
Understand the purpose of sanad
Unlike a bare objection, a supported objection includes a reason that reinforces the denial, making it harder for the opponent to dismiss.
The text: 'المنع مع السند أقوى منه'.
3
Distinguish sanad in jadal from other sciences
In hadith, sanad means chain of transmission. Here, it means any logical argument or evidence that supports an objection.
Be careful not to confuse the two meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن طلب الدليل قد يخلو عن ذكر السند كأن يقال لا نسلم ما ذكرته أو منعه أو كأن يقال هو ممنوع مطلوب البيان ولا يزاد على هذا القدر ويسمى هذا المنع في عرفهم منها مجردا. والمنع المجرد صحيح. لكن المنع مع السند أقوى منه. والسند في عرفهم ما يذكر لتقوية المنع.
Then know that requesting evidence may be devoid of mentioning the support (sanad), such as saying 'We do not accept what you mentioned' or its objection, or such as saying 'It [i.e., what you mentioned] is denied, requiring clarification,' and no more is added beyond this amount. This objection is called in their terminology 'bare' (mujarrad). The bare objection is valid. But the objection with support is stronger than it. And 'support' in their terminology is what is mentioned to strengthen the objection.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage belongs to the field of dialectic (jadal). It explains the concept of 'objection' (manع) in debate. The author first states that a request for evidence may be made without citing any 'sanad' (support), meaning the objector simply says 'We do not accept' or 'This is denied' without providing a reason. Such a bare objection is valid and accepted in the discipline. However, if the objector accompanies his objection with a supporting reason, that objection is stronger. The author then defines 'sanad' as that which is mentioned to strengthen the objection. In a fiqh debate, for example, if one scholar says 'This contract is invalid', the opponent may simply say 'لا نسلم' (we do not accept); this is a bare objection and is permissible, but it is more effective to add a supporting argument like 'because its condition is missing'.

سند
مجرد

A) To replace the objection entirely.
B) To strengthen the objection.
C) To weaken the objection.

Key Takeaway
Sanad in dialectic is a supporting argument that strengthens an objection, making it more persuasive than a bare denial.

The Meaning and Types of Naqḍ (Refutation)
Author defines naqḍ and distinguishes its general and real meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general and real (ḥaqīqī) meanings of naqḍ and identify its two specific forms: invalidation by non-occurrence of the ruling or by necessitating corruption.

Core Idea
Naqḍ is not just objecting; it is invalidating the proof by showing a logical flaw.

This passage from al-Waladiyya defines naqḍ, a key dialectical technique in jadal and munazara. The author clarifies that naqḍ has a general sense, meaning any invalidation by evidence, and a specific real sense involving direct attack on the evidence itself. Understanding this distinction is crucial for engaging in rigorous theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and context
The passage discusses the term 'naqḍ' as used in this treatise. Note that when unqualified, it has a general meaning; when specified, it refers to the real meaning.
2
Extract the general meaning
The general meaning is 'إبطال شيء بدليل' (invalidation of something by evidence) which includes any refutation that uses proof.
This is the broad application.
3
Distinguish the real (ḥaqīqī) meaning
The real meaning is specifically invalidating the evidence (dalīl) itself. This occurs either by showing that the ruling does not follow (takhalluf al-ḥukm) or by necessitating a corruption (ilzām al-fasād) in the proof.
These are the two subtypes.
4
Note the alternative definition
Some define naqḍ as invalidating evidence or definition by the corruption of what follows (tadhyīl). This is a third view mentioned by the author.
It is closely related to the second type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأينما وقع النقض في هذه الرسالة بدون قيد التفصيل سواء قيد بقيد إجمالي أولا فمعناه إبطال شيء بدليل أو ما في حكمه فيهم التنبيه وبداهة العقل. وأما معناه الحقيقي فهو إبطال الدليل بتخلف الحكم أو بإلزام الفساد. وقيل إبطال الدليل أو التعريف بفساد ما تذيل.
And wherever the term 'naqḍ' occurs in this treatise without the qualification 'detailed' (tafṣīl), whether it is qualified by a general qualification or not, its meaning is invalidation of something by evidence or what is in its ruling, implying alertness and innate reasoning. As for its real meaning, it is invalidation of the evidence by the non-occurrence of the ruling or by necessitating corruption. And it was said: invalidation of evidence or definition by the corruption of what follows.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'naqḍ' (refutation) as used in this treatise. The author explains that whenever the word 'naqḍ' appears without the specification 'tafṣīl' (detailed), it generally means 'invalidation of something by evidence or what is analogous to it,' implying intellectual alertness. The real (ḥaqīqī) meaning of naqḍ is specifically the invalidation of a proof (dalīl) either by showing that the ruling does not follow (takhalluf al-ḥukm) or by pointing out a logical flaw that necessitates the corruption of the proof. Alternatively, some say it is the invalidation of a proof or definition by the corruption of its consequence. In a kalam debate, if someone argues 'The world is eternal because it is perpetual,' the opponent may refute by saying 'If it were eternal, it would require a cause, but it does not,' which is a naqḍ.

Key Terms
نقض
إبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the real (ḥaqīqī) meaning of naqḍ according to the text?
A) A simple denial of the opponent's claim.
B) Invalidation of evidence by showing the ruling does not follow or by necessitating corruption.
C) Raising an objection without any support.
D) Asking for evidence from the opponent.

Key Takeaway
Naqḍ (refutation) in its technical sense is the invalidation of the evidence itself, not just a counterclaim, achieved by showing a logical gap or a corrupt consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Construct your own naqḍ against a simple argument: 'The Qur'an is created because it is composed of letters.' Explain which type you are using.

Objecting to Non-Exhaustive Division and the Response of Tahrir
Author explains how to object and respond to a non-exhaustive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an objection to a non-exhaustive division in a jadal debate and formulate the proper response using tahrir al-taqsim or tahrir al-muqassam.

Core Idea
The validity of a division depends on its type; rational divisions are defeated by mere possibility, inductive only by actual existence.

In jadal debates, divisions (taqsim) are frequently used to structure arguments. An opponent can challenge a division by claiming it is not exhaustive (ghayr hasir), i.e., that it leaves out some part. The skilled responder must know how to parry such objections by distinguishing the type of division and refining the terms. This lesson unpacks the method from the classical text, so you can wield this tool in your own dialectical encounters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of division being used
The passage distinguishes rational (ʿaqli), inductive (istiqraʾi), and conventional (jaʿli) or certain (qaṭʿi) divisions. Determine which type is claimed, as the response depends on it.
Rational divisions are based on conceptual possibility; inductive ones are based on actual instances.
2
Understand the objector's move
The objector posits a missing part (wāsiṭa) and claims the division is not exhaustive. This is the standard objection against any division.
The objector need not prove the part actually exists; mere possibility suffices for rational divisions.
3
Apply the relevant rule
For rational divisions, deny that the alleged part is even possible (the minor premise). For inductive divisions, deny that the missing part actually exists (the major premise).
The text states: rational division is invalidated by mere allowance of another part; inductive division only by its actual existence.
4
Use tahrir (refinement) to strengthen your response
Refine either the division (taqsim) by specifying its type, or the divided concept (muqassam) by restricting its meaning to exclude the alleged intermediate part. This blocks the objection definitively.
Tahrir al-muqassam means redefining the divided term so it cannot include the supposed missing part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باطل لأنه غير حاصر فيقول إن أردت أنه تقسيم عقلي مقارن بجواز قسم كذا فالصغرى ممنوعة وإن أردت أنه تقسيم استقرائي أو جعلي أو قطعي كذا فالكبرى ممنوعة مستندا في كل منها بتحرير التقسيم بأن هذه القسمة استقرائية أو جعلية أو قطعية. والقسم الذي جوزته غير متحقق قسميته في الواقع هذا في الأولين، أو مبين عدم قسميته بهذا الدليل هذا في الثالث. وقد يزعم أن التقسيم الاستقرائي أو الجعلي قطعيا فينقضه بأن يقول هذا التقسيم غير حاصر لمقارنته بجواز قسم آخر لم يبطل الدليل قسميته فيجاب عنه بأحد المنع المذكورة مستندا في كل منها بتحرير القسم بأن هذه القسمة استقرائية أو جعلية والقسم الذي جوزته غير متحقق قسميته في الخارج. اهـ. فإذا علمت أن التقسيم العقلي يبطل بمجرد تجويز العقل قسما آخر والاستقرائي لا يبطل إلا بتحققه. فإذا أبطلهما السائل (أي أحدهما من التقسيم العقلي والاستقرائي) بسبب عدم الحصر فقد يجيب عنه (أي عن ذلك الاعتراض) القاسم والمراد منه من ألزم صحة التقسيم سواء صدر عنه التقسيم أولا. قبل إنما قال قاسما ولم يقل مقسما مع أن قوله تقدما تقتضيه لما اشتهر من أن ماضي التقسيم لم يأت مشددا بل مخففا وفيه نظر لأنه دعوى بلا دليل بل هو واقع قال في القاموس قسمه وقسمه مخففا ومشددا. مستندا بتحرير المراد من المقسم وهو أعم من أن يكون مقسم التقسيم العقلي مطلقا أو مقسم التقسيم الاستقرائي كذلك. أعني من تحرير المقسم أن يريد منه (أي من المقسم) معنى لا يشمل تلك الواسطة بالإمكان أو بالفعل. وقد عرفت معنى الواسطة.

He says: If you mean it is a rational division comparable to the permissibility of such a part, then the minor premise is denied; if you mean it is an inductive, conventional, or certain division, then the major premise is denied, relying in each on refining the division: that this division is inductive, conventional, or certain. The part whose existence you posited is not actually realized as a division of it - this in the first two; or its division is shown not to be due to this evidence - this in the third. One may claim that the inductive or conventional division is certain, so he objects by saying: This division is not exhaustive because it is comparable to the permissibility of another part that does not invalidate the evidence of its division. He is answered by one of the mentioned denials, relying in each on refining the division: that this division is inductive or conventional, and the part whose existence you posited is not actually realized as a division of it externally. End. So if you know that the rational division is invalidated by the mere mental allowance of another part, while the inductive is not invalidated except by its realization. So if the objector defeats both (i.e., either the rational or the inductive) due to lack of exhaustiveness, the divider may answer (i.e., about that objection), and what is meant by 'the divider' is the one who asserts the validity of the division, whether he originally made the division or not. It was said: He only said 'divider' and did not say 'divided' even though his previous statement requires it, because it is well-known that the past tense of 'division' does not come with shaddah but with tashdid? Actually with tashdid is weaker. There is a view because it is a claim without proof, but it is a reality. They said in al-Qamus: 'qasama' and 'qasama' are both with tashdid and without. Relying on refining what is meant by 'the divided' (al-muqassam), which is more general than being the divider of absolute rational division or of inductive division likewise. That is, by refining the divided that he intends from it (i.e., from the divided) a meaning that does not include that intermediary (wāsiṭa) by possibility or actuality. And you already know the meaning of intermediary.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage, from a work on jadal (dialectics), treats the method of objecting to a division (taqsim) by pointing out that it is non-exhaustive (ghayr hasir), and the proper response. The discussion distinguishes three types of division: rational ('aqli), inductive (istiqra'i), and conventional (ja'li) or certain (qat'i). The objector may claim the division is not exhaustive because another part is possible. The responder can deny either the minor premise (if the division is rational, denying that the alleged missing part is possible) or the major premise (if the division is inductive, denying that all non-exhaustive divisions are invalid). The key move is 'tahrir al-taqsim' (refining the division): specifying that the division is of a particular type, and that the supposed missing part is not actually a valid division of the original concept. The passage then states a critical rule: a rational division is invalidated merely by the intellect's allowance of another part, while an inductive division is invalidated only by actual existence of that part. The responder may also refine the divided concept (muqassam) itself: he restricts its meaning so that it does not include the intermediate case (wasiṭa) that the objector posits. This is a standard jadal technique to preserve the division against objections of incompleteness. The commentary on the word 'qasim' versus 'muqassim' is a linguistic aside.

The debater's response to a charge of non-exhaustive division depends on specifying the division type and refining the terms used.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
10 min
Responding to an Objection of Non-Exhaustiveness
Example of dividing the known and dialectical responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three main strategies for responding to a charge of non-exhaustiveness in a division, using the example of dividing 'the known' into existent and non-existent.

Core Idea
Non-exhaustiveness can be countered by specifying the division type, refining the divided concept, or denying exhaustive intent.

In dialectical debates (jadal), a common objection is that a division misses a part. Using the example of 'the known' divided into existent and non-existent, the objector proposes the 'state' (hal) as an intermediate. This lesson analyses the responder's possible replies, which vary with the division type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
The objector asserts the division is non-exhaustive because it omits the state (hal). He argues that the division is comparable to (muqārin) the possibility of another part, hence invalid.
The objection assumes the division aims at exhaustiveness.
2
First response: deny based on division type
If the division is rational, deny the minor premise (the state is not a possible part). If inductive, deny the major premise (non-exhaustiveness invalidates only when the missing part is actual).
This relies on 'tahrir al-qism' (refining the division).
3
Second response: refine the divided concept
Redefine 'the known' to exclude the state, or broaden 'existent' or 'non-existent' to include it. This denies that the state is truly outside the parts.
Also called 'tahrir al-muqassam'.
4
Third response: deny exhaustive intent
The divider may claim he does not intend exhaustive coverage. This is a direct denial of the major premise that every non-exhaustive division is invalid.
Only available if the division is not meant to be exhaustive.
