في الصغرى فيقول إن أردت أنه تقسيم عقلي مقارن بجواز قسم كذا فالصغرى ممنوعة، وإن أردت أنه تقسيم استقرائي أو جعلي أو قطعي كذا فالكبرى ممنوعة مستندا في كل منها بتحرير القسم بأن هذه القسمة استقرائية أو جعلية والقسم الذي جوزته غير متحقق قسمته في الواقع هذا في الأولين أو مبين عدم قسمته بهذا الدليل هذا في الثالث. وقد يزعم أن التقسيم الاستقرائي أو الجعلي قطعيا فينقضه بأن يقول هذا التقسيم غير حاصر بتحرير المقسم أعني به بيان أن يريد منه معنى لا يشمل الواسطة بالإمكان أو بالفعل أو بتحرير القسم الآخر بحيث يظهر به عدم جواز دخوله أو عدم تحقق دخوله. حاصر لأقسامه لأنه مقارن بجواز قسم آخر داخل في القسم غير داخل في الأقسام لأنه لا يشمل الحال الذي هو لا موجود ولا معدوم. وكل شيء شأنه هذا فهو غير حاصر فيكون باطلا. فيجيب عنه صاحب التقسيم بمنع الصغرى بأنا لا نسلم أنه مقارن بجواز قسم آخر داخل في القسم. لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من المعلوم معنى لا يشمل الحال. ولو سلم أنه داخل في المقسم فلا نسلم أنه غير داخل في الأقسام. لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من المعدوم أو الموجود معنى شاملا للحال. وقد يجاب بمنع كبرى أصل الدليل بأنا لا نسلم أن كل غير حاصر فهو باطل. لم لا يجوز أن لا يكون مراده الحصر. تأمل وقس عليه التقسيم الاستقرائي. والجواب عن الاعتراض الوارد على التقسيم الجعلي كالجواب عن الاستقرائي. والجواب عن الاعتراض الوارد على التقسيم القطعي كالجواب عن العقلي. وقد يجاب عنه بتحرير الأقسام ومادة القضية وتغير التقسيم وبالنقض. والله رضة التحة قبيين [؟] وأشار إلى هذا بقيد للقيمة الجزاة [؟] تأمل في هذا المقام واستخرج الاعتراض الوارد على التقسيم العقلي الحقيقي والاعتباري والتقسيم الاستقرائي الحقيقي والاعتباري والقطعي والجعلي، والجواب عن كل منها. واعلم أن كون الاعتراض من السائل النقض فقط إذا لم تعتبر الدعوى الضمنية، وأما إذا اعتبرت فيرد عليه المنع المجازي اللغوي والمعارضة التقديرية أيضا هذا على مذهب المحقق الشرف قدس سره من أن التقسيم من المطالب التصورية، وأما على ما حققه التفتازاني من أن التقسيم من المطالب التصديقية فيرد عليه المنع المجازي اللغوي مطلقا والمعارضة التقديرية والنقض شيبها أو تحقيقيا وقس عليه الجواب عن كل منها. [فائدة] إن قيل وجه الحصر فإنه يدل على الحصر العقلي، وإن قيل وجه الضبط فإنه يدل على الاستقرائي. قال: اعلم أن الحصر أربعة أقسام: حصر عقلي كحصر العدد في الزوج والفرد، وحصر وضعي كحصر الكلمة في الثلاث، وحصر جعلي كحصر الرسالة في المقدمة والمقالات الثلاثة والخاتمة، وحصر استقرائي كحصر الأبواب والفصول. ولما فرغ من الاعتراض على التقسيم بانتفاء الشرط الأول شرع في الاعتراض عليه بانتفاء الشرط الثاني، فلذلك أورده في فصل على حدة ساق التقسيم لكن لما كان بعض الاعتراض بانتفاء الشرط الثالث مناسبا لبعض الاعتراض بانتفاء الشرط الثاني في تعبير الفساد إذ التعبير في أحدهما كون قسم الشيء قسما له وفي الآخر كون قسيم الشيء قباله جمع بينهما في هذا الفصل. فإن قلت لم قدم الاعتراض باعتبار انتفاء الشرط الثالث على الثاني مع أن الأولى عكسه القريب للفصل الآتي المسوق للاعتراض بانتفاء الشرط في القسم أو يمنع القضية الثالثة أعني خروج ذلك القسم عن الأقسام. وقد يجاب عنه بمنع كلية الكبرى مستندا بأن هذه القسمة مقارنة بإرادة عدم الحصر فافهم. والجواب عن الاعتراض الوارد على التقسيم الجملي كالجواب عن الاستقرائي. والجواب عن الاعتراض على التقسيم القطعي كالجواب عن العقلي إلا أنه قد يجاب فيه بمنع القضية الرابعة مستندا بوجود دليل يدل على بطلان دخول ذلك القسم في المقسم لأن الصغرى من دليل النقض الوارد على التقسيم القطعي مشتملة على أربع مقدمات بخلاف الثلاثة الباقية، فإن كلا من صغريات دليل النقض الوارد عليها مشتملة على ثلاث قضايا فقط فاعرف.
For example, if we say 'The known is either existent or non-existent', then it is objected that this division is invalid because it is not exhaustive due to the possibility of another part, which is the state (hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent. The divider responds to this objection by denying the universality of the major premise or by equivocating on the minor premise, saying: If you mean it is a rational division comparable to the permissibility of such a part, then the minor premise is denied; if you mean it is an inductive, conventional, or certain division, then the major premise is denied, relying in each on refining the division: that this division is inductive or conventional, and the part whose existence you posited is not actually realized as a division of it in reality - this in the first two; or showing that it is not a division by this evidence - this in the third. One may claim that the inductive or conventional division is certain, so he objects by saying: This division is not exhaustive because it is comparable to the permissibility of another part that does not invalidate the evidence of its division. He is answered by one of the mentioned denials, relying in each on refining the division: that this division is inductive or conventional, and the part whose existence you posited is not actually realized as a division of it externally. So if you know that the rational division is invalidated by the mere mental allowance of another part, while the inductive is not invalidated except by its realization. So if the objector defeats both (i.e., either the rational or the inductive) due to lack of exhaustiveness, the divider may answer by denying the first proposition among the premises that the minor premise led to, i.e., the permissibility of the existence of the other part or its realization, relying on refining that part so that its impermissibility or lack of realization appears; or by denying the second proposition, i.e., the permissibility of its entering or the realization of its entering into the divided, relying on refining the divided - meaning to clarify that he intends from it a meaning that does not include the intermediary by possibility or actuality; or by refining the other part so that its impermissibility of entering or lack of realization appears. (The objection continues:) It is not exhaustive because it is comparable to the permissibility of another part that is inside the division but not included in the parts, because it does not include the state that is neither existent nor non-existent. And everything of this nature is non-exhaustive, so it is invalid. The one who made the division responds by denying the minor: We do not accept that it is comparable to the permissibility of another part inside the division. It is not permissible that what is meant by 'the known' is a meaning that does not include the state. Even if it is admitted that it is inside the divided, we do not accept that it is not inside the parts. Why is it not permissible that what is meant by 'non-existent' or 'existent' is a meaning inclusive of the state? And it may be answered by denying the major of the original argument: We do not accept that every non-exhaustive division is invalid. Why is it not permissible that he does not intend exhaustiveness? Reflect, and apply analogously to inductive division. The answer to the objection raised against conventional division is like the answer against inductive division. The answer to the objection against certain division is like the answer against rational division. It may also be answered by refining the parts, the matter of the proposition, changing the division, and by counter-objection. And God knows best [?]. He alluded to this by the restriction of the value intention [?]. Reflect on this place and extract the objection raised against real rational division, conventional rational division, real inductive division, conventional inductive division, certain division, and conventional division, and the answer to each. Know that the objection from the objector is only a counter-objection (naqd) if the implicit claim is not considered. But if it is considered, then figurative linguistic denial (man' majazi lughawi) and hypothetical counter-argument (mu'arada taqdiriya) also apply. This is according to the school of al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif (may his secret be sanctified) that division is among tasawwuri demands. But according to al-Taftazani's verification that division is among tasdiqi demands, figurative linguistic denial applies absolutely, and hypothetical counter-argument and counter-objection in the form of doubt or certainty apply. Apply analogously the answer to each. [Benefit] If 'exhaustiveness' (hasr) is said, it indicates rational exhaustiveness; if 'control' (dabt) is said, it indicates inductive exhaustiveness. He said: Know that exhaustiveness is of four types: rational exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of number into even and odd; verbal exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of the word into three; conventional (ja'li) exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of the epistle into the introduction, three treatises, and conclusion; and inductive exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of chapters and sections. When he finished with the objection to division by the negation of the first condition, he began with the objection to it by the negation of the second condition. Therefore, he presented it in a separate chapter following the division. However, since some objection by the negation of the third condition is suitable to some objection by the negation of the second condition in expressing the corruption, because the expression in one is that a part of a thing is a part of it, and in the other that the co-division is opposite to it, he combined them in this chapter. If you say: Why did he present the objection considering the negation of the third condition before the second, while the first is the reverse? The immediate reverse is for the next chapter, which is meant for the objection by negation of the condition in the division? Or he denies the third proposition, i.e., the exit of that part from the parts. It may be answered by denying the universality of the major, relying on that this division is comparable to the intention of not being exhaustive. Understand. The answer to the objection raised against total (jumali) division is like the answer against inductive division. The answer to the objection against certain division is like the answer against rational division, except that it may be answered by denying the fourth proposition, relying on the existence of evidence indicating the invalidity of that part's entry into the divided, because the minor premise of the counter-objection argument for certain division consists of four premises, unlike the remaining three, for each of the minors of the counter-objection argument that applies consists of only three propositions. So know.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical discussion of division, using a concrete example: dividing 'the known' (al-ma'lum) into 'existent' (mawjud) and 'non-existent' (ma'dum). The objector points out a missing part: the 'state' (hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent. The responder has multiple strategies. First, he can deny the major premise (that every non-exhaustive division is invalid) or equivocate on the minor premise by specifying the type of division. If it is a rational division, he denies that the state is possible; if inductive, he denies that the state actually exists. Second, he can refine the divided concept (muqassam): by restricting the meaning of 'the known' to exclude the state, or by arguing that the state is actually included in one of the existing parts (e.g., 'non-existent') if their meanings are broadened. Third, he can outright deny that the division is intended to be exhaustive. The passage then compares responses for different division types: conventional (ja'li) division follows inductive, certain (qat'i) follows rational. It notes that certain division has an additional fourth premise in the objection, requiring a denial of that premise with evidence. A benefit (fa'ida) follows, listing four types of exhaustiveness (hasr): rational (like numbers into odd/even), verbal (like word into three letters), conventional (like an epistle into introduction/treatises/conclusion), and inductive (like chapters of a book). Finally, the author transitions to objections based on the second condition of division (that the parts be truly parts of the whole), and explains the arrangement of objections. The passage requires careful parsing of the dialectical moves: understanding the types of division, the structure of objections, and the appropriate responses. The example illustrates how to handle intermediate cases that challenge a dichotomy.

Checkpoint
In the example of dividing 'the known' into existent and non-existent, what does the objector propose as a missing part?
A) A part that is both existent and non-existent.
B) A part that is neither existent nor non-existent (the state).
C) A part that is possibly non-existent.
D) A part that is absolutely non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The key dialectical move is that a charge of non-exhaustiveness can be countered by specifying the division type, refining the divided concept, or disclaiming exhaustive intent.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Types of Exhaustiveness and Their Responses
Benefit on four types of exhaustiveness and their responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of exhaustiveness (rational, verbal, conventional, inductive) and explain how the response to an objection depends on the type.

Core Idea
The type of exhaustiveness determines what invalidates a division and how to defend it.

The passage includes a 'benefit' that lists four types of exhaustiveness (hasr): rational, verbal, conventional, and inductive. Each has different conditions for exhaustiveness, and thus different vulnerabilities to objection. This lesson parses these types and the appropriate responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
The text names rational (e.g., number into even/odd), verbal (word into three), conventional (epistle parts), and inductive (book chapters).
Each type has its own definition of exhaustiveness.
2
Understand the condition for invalidation
Rational exhaustiveness is defeated by mere mental possibility of another part. Inductive requires actual existence. Conventional depends on the maker's intention. Verbal depends on linguistic convention.
This matches the earlier claim that rational division is invalidated by imagining another part, inductive by its actuality.
3
Map response strategies to each type
For rational: deny the possibility of the missing part. For inductive: deny its actuality. For conventional: deny that it is intended as part. For verbal: rely on linguistic usage.
Certain division (qat'i) is treated like rational but with an extra premise.
4
Apply to given examples
Practice classifying divisions and determining valid objections and responses.
Use Islamic examples like the division of water into pure and impure, or the parts of a sermon.

غير داخل في الأقسام لأنه لا يشمل الحال الذي هو لا موجود ولا معدوم. وكل شيء شأنه هذا فهو غير حاصر فيكون باطلا. فيجيب عنه صاحب التقسيم بمنع الصغرى بأنا لا نسلم أنه مقارن بجواز قسم آخر داخل في القسم. لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من المعلوم معنى لا يشمل الحال. ولو سلم أنه داخل في المقسم فلا نسلم أنه غير داخل في الأقسام. لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من المعدوم أو الموجود معنى شاملا للحال. وقد يجاب بمنع كبرى أصل الاعتراض الوارد على التقسيم القطعي كالجواب عن العقلي. وقد يجاب عنه بتحرير الأقسام ومادة القضية وتغير التقسيم وبالنقض. والله رضة التحة قبيين [؟] وأشار إلى هذا بقيد للقيمة الجزاة [؟] تأمل في هذا المقام واستخرج الاعتراض الوارد على التقسيم العقلي الحقيقي والاعتباري والتقسيم الاستقرائي الحقيقي والاعتباري والقطعي والجعلي، والجواب عن كل منها. واعلم أن كون الاعتراض من السائل النقض فقط إذا لم تعتبر الدعوى الضمنية، وأما إذا اعتبرت فيرد عليه المنع المجازي اللغوي والمعارضة التقديرية أيضا هذا على مذهب المحقق الشرف قدس سره من أن التقسيم من المطالب التصورية، وأما على ما حققه التفتازاني من أن التقسيم من المطالب التصديقية فيرد عليه المنع المجازي اللغوي مطلقا والمعارضة التقديرية والنقض شيبها أو تحقيقيا وقس عليه الجواب عن كل منها. [فائدة] إن قيل وجه الحصر فإنه يدل على الحصر العقلي، وإن قيل وجه الضبط فإنه يدل على الاستقرائي. قال: اعلم أن الحصر أربعة أقسام: حصر عقلي كحصر العدد في الزوج والفرد، وحصر وضعي كحصر الكلمة في الثلاث، وحصر جعلي كحصر الرسالة في المقدمة والمقالات الثلاثة والخاتمة، وحصر استقرائي كحصر الأبواب والفصول. ولما فرغ من الاعتراض على التقسيم بانتفاء الشرط الأول شرع في الاعتراض عليه بانتفاء الشرط الثاني، فلذلك أورده في فصل على حدة ساق التقسيم لكن لما كان بعض الاعتراض بانتفاء الشرط الثالث مناسبا لبعض الاعتراض بانتفاء الشرط الثاني في تعبير الفساد إذ التعبير في أحدهما كون قسم الشيء قسما له وفي الآخر كون قسيم الشيء قباله جمع بينهما في هذا الفصل. فإن قلت لم قدم الاعتراض باعتبار انتفاء الشرط الثالث على الثاني مع أن الأولى عكسه القريب للفصل الآتي المسوق للاعتراض بانتفاء الشرط في القسم أو يمنع القضية الثالثة أعني خروج ذلك القسم عن الأقسام. وقد يجاب عنه بمنع كلية الكبرى مستندا بأن هذه القسمة مقارنة بإرادة عدم الحصر فافهم. والجواب عن الاعتراض الوارد على التقسيم الجملي كالجواب عن الاستقرائي. والجواب عن الاعتراض على التقسيم القطعي كالجواب عن العقلي إلا أنه قد يجاب فيه بمنع القضية الرابعة مستندا بوجود دليل يدل على بطلان دخول ذلك القسم في المقسم لأن الصغرى من دليل النقض الوارد على التقسيم القطعي مشتملة على أربع مقدمات بخلاف الثلاثة الباقية، فإن كلا من صغريات دليل النقض الوارد عليها مشتملة على ثلاث قضايا فقط فاعرف.
For example, if we say 'The known is either existent or non-existent', then it is objected that this division is invalid because it is not exhaustive due to the possibility of another part, which is the state (hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent. The divider responds to this objection by denying the universality of the major premise or by equivocating on the minor premise, saying: If you mean it is a rational division comparable to the permissibility of such a part, then the minor premise is denied; if you mean it is an inductive, conventional, or certain division, then the major premise is denied, relying in each on refining the division: that this division is inductive or conventional, and the part whose existence you posited is not actually realized as a division of it in reality - this in the first two; or showing that it is not a division by this evidence - this in the third. One may claim that the inductive or conventional division is certain, so he objects by saying: This division is not exhaustive because it is comparable to the permissibility of another part that does not invalidate the evidence of its division. He is answered by one of the mentioned denials, relying in each on refining the division: that this division is inductive or conventional, and the part whose existence you posited is not actually realized as a division of it externally. So if you know that the rational division is invalidated by the mere mental allowance of another part, while the inductive is not invalidated except by its realization. So if the objector defeats both (i.e., either the rational or the inductive) due to lack of exhaustiveness, the divider may answer by denying the first proposition among the premises that the minor premise led to, i.e., the permissibility of the existence of the other part or its realization, relying on refining that part so that its impermissibility or lack of realization appears; or by denying the second proposition, i.e., the permissibility of its entering or the realization of its entering into the divided, relying on refining the divided - meaning to clarify that he intends from it a meaning that does not include the intermediary by possibility or actuality; or by refining the other part so that its impermissibility of entering or lack of realization appears. (The objection continues:) It is not exhaustive because it is comparable to the permissibility of another part that is inside the division but not included in the parts, because it does not include the state that is neither existent nor non-existent. And everything of this nature is non-exhaustive, so it is invalid. The one who made the division responds by denying the minor: We do not accept that it is comparable to the permissibility of another part inside the division. It is not permissible that what is meant by 'the known' is a meaning that does not include the state. Even if it is admitted that it is inside the divided, we do not accept that it is not inside the parts. Why is it not permissible that what is meant by 'non-existent' or 'existent' is a meaning inclusive of the state? And it may be answered by denying the major of the original argument: We do not accept that every non-exhaustive division is invalid. Why is it not permissible that he does not intend exhaustiveness? Reflect, and apply analogously to inductive division. The answer to the objection raised against conventional division is like the answer against inductive division. The answer to the objection against certain division is like the answer against rational division. It may also be answered by refining the parts, the matter of the proposition, changing the division, and by counter-objection. And God knows best [?]. He alluded to this by the restriction of the value intention [?]. Reflect on this place and extract the objection raised against real rational division, conventional rational division, real inductive division, conventional inductive division, certain division, and conventional division, and the answer to each. Know that the objection from the objector is only a counter-objection (naqd) if the implicit claim is not considered. But if it is considered, then figurative linguistic denial (man' majazi lughawi) and hypothetical counter-argument (mu'arada taqdiriya) also apply. This is according to the school of al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif (may his secret be sanctified) that division is among tasawwuri demands. But according to al-Taftazani's verification that division is among tasdiqi demands, figurative linguistic denial applies absolutely, and hypothetical counter-argument and counter-objection in the form of doubt or certainty apply. Apply analogously the answer to each. [Benefit] If 'exhaustiveness' (hasr) is said, it indicates rational exhaustiveness; if 'control' (dabt) is said, it indicates inductive exhaustiveness. He said: Know that exhaustiveness is of four types: rational exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of number into even and odd; verbal exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of the word into three; conventional (ja'li) exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of the epistle into the introduction, three treatises, and conclusion; and inductive exhaustiveness, like the exhaustiveness of chapters and sections. When he finished with the objection to division by the negation of the first condition, he began with the objection to it by the negation of the second condition. Therefore, he presented it in a separate chapter following the division. However, since some objection by the negation of the third condition is suitable to some objection by the negation of the second condition in expressing the corruption, because the expression in one is that a part of a thing is a part of it, and in the other that the co-division is opposite to it, he combined them in this chapter. If you say: Why did he present the objection considering the negation of the third condition before the second, while the first is the reverse? The immediate reverse is for the next chapter, which is meant for the objection by negation of the condition in the division? Or he denies the third proposition, i.e., the exit of that part from the parts. It may be answered by denying the universality of the major, relying on that this division is comparable to the intention of not being exhaustive. Understand. The answer to the objection raised against total (jumali) division is like the answer against inductive division. The answer to the objection against certain division is like the answer against rational division, except that it may be answered by denying the fourth proposition, relying on the existence of evidence indicating the invalidity of that part's entry into the divided, because the minor premise of the counter-objection argument for certain division consists of four premises, unlike the remaining three, for each of the minors of the counter-objection argument that applies consists of only three propositions. So know.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical discussion of division, using a concrete example: dividing 'the known' (al-ma'lum) into 'existent' (mawjud) and 'non-existent' (ma'dum). The objector points out a missing part: the 'state' (hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent. The responder has multiple strategies. First, he can deny the major premise (that every non-exhaustive division is invalid) or equivocate on the minor premise by specifying the type of division. If it is a rational division, he denies that the state is possible; if inductive, he denies that the state actually exists. Second, he can refine the divided concept (muqassam): by restricting the meaning of 'the known' to exclude the state, or by arguing that the state is actually included in one of the existing parts (e.g., 'non-existent') if their meanings are broadened. Third, he can outright deny that the division is intended to be exhaustive. The passage then compares responses for different division types: conventional (ja'li) division follows inductive, certain (qat'i) follows rational. It notes that certain division has an additional fourth premise in the objection, requiring a denial of that premise with evidence. A benefit (fa'ida) follows, listing four types of exhaustiveness (hasr): rational (like numbers into odd/even), verbal (like word into three letters), conventional (like an epistle into introduction/treatises/conclusion), and inductive (like chapters of a book). Finally, the author transitions to objections based on the second condition of division (that the parts be truly parts of the whole), and explains the arrangement of objections. The passage requires careful parsing of the dialectical moves: understanding the types of division, the structure of objections, and the appropriate responses. The example illustrates how to handle intermediate cases that challenge a dichotomy.

الحصر الوضعي
الحصر الجعلي
الحصر الاستقرائي

Objecting to a Division by Negating Its Conditions
Author explains how to object to a division by negating each of its three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formulate an objection (naqd) against a division by negating any of its three conditions: hasr, tanaqud, and man' al-aghyar.

Core Idea
A valid division requires completeness, exclusivity, and no extraneous parts; negate any one to object.

In jadal (dialectical debate), the validity of a division can be challenged by showing that it violates one of three essential conditions. This passage teaches you how to raise such objections systematically. Understanding these conditions sharpens your critical analysis of any classificatory scheme used in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division under scrutiny
Locate the divided thing (al-muqassam) and the listed parts (al-aqsam).
2
Check the first condition: Hasr (completeness)
Are all true parts of the divided thing included? If a part is missing, the division is incomplete.
Example: If you divide 'animals' into 'human' and 'horse', you miss 'bird'.
3
Check the second condition: Tanaqud (mutual exclusivity)
Do any of the listed parts share members? If they overlap, the division is invalid.
Example: Dividing 'people' into 'male' and 'female' is exclusive, but 'adult' and 'child' overlaps.
4
Check the third condition: Man' al-aghyar (preventing extraneous parts)
Is every listed part truly a part of the divided thing? If a listed part is not actually a part, the division includes extraneous elements.
Example: Dividing 'legal rulings' into 'wajib', 'haram', and 'sleep' is extraneous because sleep is not a ruling.
5
Formulate the objection
Use phrases like: 'This division is invalid because it fails to include [missing part] / because [part A] and [part B] overlap / because [listed part] is not actually within the divided thing.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستخرج الاعتراض عليه أى هذا التقسيم بانتفاء الشرط الأول والثاني والثالث . أما النقض بانتفاء الأول فبأن يقال هذا التقسيم باطل لأنه غير حاصل لأقسامه لوجود قسم آخر داخل في المقسم خارج عن الأقسام، وبانتفاء الثاني بأن فيه تصادق الأقسام ، وبانتفاء الثالث بأنه غير مانع الأغياره لأن القسم الفلاني داخل في الأقسام غير داخل القسم
And extract the objection against it, i.e., this division, by the negation of the first, second, and third conditions. As for the refutation by negation of the first, it is by saying: This division is invalid because it does not achieve its parts due to the existence of another part within the divided thing outside the mentioned parts. And by negation of the second, by saying: There is overlap among the parts. And by negation of the third, by saying: It does not prevent extraneous elements, because such-and-such part is within the divisions but not within the divided thing.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage demonstrates how to raise objections (naqd) against a division by showing that it violates one of the three conditions previously established. The first objection targets incompleteness: a division is invalid if there exists a part of the divided thing that is not listed among the divisions. The second objection targets overlap: if the divisions have common members, the division fails because the parts are not mutually exclusive. The third objection targets inclusion of extraneous elements: if a listed division is not actually a part of the divided thing, the division is invalid. In dialectical practice (jadal), these objections are used to challenge an opponent's classification.

Key Terms
نقض
انتفاء الشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does one object to a division using the first condition?
A) By showing the parts overlap.
B) By showing a part of the divided thing is omitted.
C) By showing an extraneous part is included.
D) By showing the division is not real.

Key Takeaway
Objecting to a division by negating a condition is a precise logical tool: if any of the three conditions (hasr, tanaqud, man' al-aghyar) is violated, the division is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the division: 'Islamic legal rulings are either wājib (obligatory) or ĥarām (prohibited).' Formulate an objection using each of the three conditions. For the first condition, note that the division omits mandūb (recommended), makrūh (disliked), and mubāĥ (permissible). For the second, wājib and ĥarām are exclusive, so no objection. For the third, both are genuine rulings, so no objection. Thus the only valid objection is on completeness.
?
In a jadal session, if your opponent divides 'knowledge' into 'acquired (kasbī)' and 'necessary (ḍarūrī)', what objection would you raise if you think it is incomplete? Write your response as: 'This division is invalid because it does not achieve its parts due to the existence of another part within the divided thing outside the mentioned parts, namely [missing part].'

Rules for Rejection and Definition of Mukabara
Author states when a questioner may reject a claim and defines obstinate rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a questioner may validly reject a claim in dialectic and distinguish it from obstinate rejection (mukabara).

Core Idea
In dialectic, you may reject only what is unproven and not self-evident; rejecting the obvious is mukabara.

The Waldiyya teaches the etiquette of scholarly debate. A key move is rejection (man'), but it is not arbitrary. The author sets precise conditions: the claim must lack proof and must not be self-evident to the questioner. If the claim is clearly self-evident, rejection becomes obstinate disputation (mukabara), which is a serious breach of dialectical ethics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for valid rejection
Read the passage carefully. The author says a questioner may reject a claim if (1) it is not accompanied by a proof (dalil), and (2) it is not self-evident (badihi jali) to the questioner, either in reality or by ruling.
Condition (2) includes hidden self-evidents (badihi khafi) and theoretical propositions that are not known by the appropriate knowledge.
2
Distinguish 'in reality' vs 'by ruling'
The text says 'not self-evident in reality nor by ruling.' 'By ruling' means something that, although not inherently self-evident, is treated as self-evident due to consensus or other factors. For example, in a fiqh debate, a ruling based on a clear text is considered self-evident by ruling.
3
Recognize the definition of mukabara
Mukabara is defined as 'disputation in a scientific issue not to manifest the truth but to silence the opponent and show off.' It is a violation of the purpose of debate, which is to seek truth.
Mukabara is not just any rejection; it is specifically the rejection of a self-evident claim without valid reason.
4
Apply the rule to a sample debate scenario
Consider a debate in fiqh: Someone claims 'Prayer is obligatory for every adult Muslim.' The proof is a clear Qur'anic verse. If a questioner rejects this claim without proof, it is mukabara because the claim is self-evident by ruling. If the claim were a disputed ruling (e.g., a specific inheritance share), rejection would be valid if no proof is provided.
This illustrates how the rule operates in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم يكن ذلك التصديق مقرونا بدليل ولم يكن بالنسبة إلى السائل بديهيا جليا لا حقيقة ولا حكما بأن يكون بديهيا خفيا أو نظريا ليسا بمعلومين بالعلم المناسب للمطلوب ولا مسلمين حقيقة، فللسائل أن يمنعه مطلقا. وإن كان ذلك التصديق بديهيا جليا حقيقة أو حكما فلا يصح منعه ويسمى منعه مكابرة. والمكابرة هي المنازعة في المسألة العلمية لا لإظهار الحق بل لاسكات الخصم وإظهار الفضل.
If that assent is not accompanied by a proof and is not self-evident to the questioner, neither in reality nor by ruling, such that it is a hidden self-evident or a theoretical [proposition] not known by the appropriate knowledge for the sought [conclusion] and not conceded in reality, then the questioner may reject it absolutely. If that assent is self-evident, in reality or by ruling, then its rejection is not valid, and its rejection is called mukabara. Mukabara is disputation in a scientific issue not to manifest the truth but to silence the opponent and show off.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from the Waldiyya explains the etiquette of debate (adab al-bahth). The key concept is 'man'' (rejection), which is a move in dialectic where the questioner refuses to accept a claim unless it is proven. The passage specifies two conditions for valid rejection: first, the claim must not be accompanied by a proof (dalil), and second, it must not be self-evident (badihi jali) to the questioner. If the claim is self-evident, rejecting it is not allowed; it is called mukabara (obstinacy). Mukabara is defined as disputation aimed at silencing the opponent rather than seeking truth. This is a serious violation of dialectical ethics. In fiqh debates, for example, one cannot reject a clear Qur'anic text; doing so would be mukabara.

Key Terms
المنع
المكابرة
بديهي جلي

Key Takeaway
Valid rejection requires absence of proof and lack of self-evidence; rejecting the self-evident is mukabara.

The Three Functions of the Questioner: Man', Mu'arada, Naqd
Author lists three allowable objections when a proposition is supported by a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and prioritize the three allowable objections (man', mu'arada, naqd) in a jadal debate and explain why each has its rank.

Core Idea
The safest objection denies a premise; the strongest challenges the claim itself.

In dialectical debate, when your opponent supports a claim with a proof, you have three legitimate moves: deny a premise, counter the claim, or refute the proof. The classical jadal tradition gives each a distinct function and priority. This passage from al-Waladiyya explains them by reasoning from safety, logical priority, and strength of interference.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the three functions
List the three objections: man' (denial of a premise), mu'arada (counter-objection to the claim), naqd (refutation of the proof). Note the order given by the author: man', then mu'arada, then naqd.
This order reflects descending priority: man' is most recommended, then mu'arada, then naqd.
2
Understand why man' is safest
The author says man' is 'aslam al-turuq' (safest way) and 'āri' 'an shā'ibat al-ghasb' (free from any taint of oppression). It targets a part (a premise), which is naturally prior to the whole, and forces the opponent to prove the denied premise, revealing truth if they succeed.
Man' avoids direct attack on the opponent's claim, making it the most measured and effective objection.
3
Understand mu'arada's priority over naqd
Mu'arada challenges the claim itself, which is the primary objective. Naqd attacks only the proof. Since interfering with the main objective is stronger than interfering with a means, mu'arada is prioritized.
This prioritization depends on the view that the claim is the main focus. Some later texts reversed this order.
4
Define naqd as the third option
Naqd attacks the logical form or validity of the proof without necessarily denying premises or the claim. It is the least preferred because it deals with the proof, not the claim or a part of the proof.
In this text, naqd is considered the weakest objection, though still legitimate.
5
Apply the priority rule in a debate scenario
Given a claim with a proof, decide which objection to use first. The ideal sequence is: if you can deny a premise, do that; if not, counter the claim; if neither, attack the proof's form.
Real debates may require tact; this is the theoretical hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if that assent is accompanied by a proof, then the questioner has three functions: first, al-man' (prevention), i.e., denying a certain premise from the premises of his proof, prioritized because it is the safest way and free from any taint of oppression, and because it pertains to a part of the proof, and the part is prior to the whole by nature, and because it is more conducive to revealing correctness, since the one being [challenged] must prove what the questioner denied, and upon proof the truth of his claim becomes apparent, unlike the last two, so ponder. Second, al-mu'arada (counter-objection), prioritized before al-naqd because it bears upon the claim itself according to the preferred view, and the claim is the primary objective, while al-naqd bears upon the proof, and interference with the primary objective is stronger than interference with the proof, so it is stronger and thus prioritized. Third, al-naqd (refutation). And since the matter is thus, there are here three articles for explaining the three functions in order. The first article is on the explanation of al-man' in absolute terms and its locus. And when he wanted to explain what may serve as the locus of man' and what may not, he emphasized it, saying: Know that...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical lesson on the three legitimate objections a questioner may raise when the opponent's claim is supported by a proof. The author enumerates them in order of strength and priority. First is 'al-man' (prevention), which denies a specific premise of the proof. This is considered the safest approach because it avoids overt conflict and focuses on a part, which is naturally prior to the whole. Moreover, it effectively tests the claim: if the opponent cannot prove the denied premise, the argument collapses. Second is 'al-mu'arada' (counter-objection), which directly challenges the claim itself, not the proof. The author argues that because the claim is the main objective, an objection to it is stronger than an objection to the proof, so mu'arada is prioritized over naqd. Third is 'al-naqd' (refutation), which attacks the logical structure or validity of the proof. The author then announces that the text will contain three articles corresponding to these three functions, beginning with an article on man'.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the most effective objection is to deny a premise of the proof, then to counter the claim, and finally to refute the proof's form.

Three Functions of the Questioner in Dialectic
Author states the three functions of the questioner and their order of priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the three functions of the questioner in dialectical debate (man', mu'āraḍa, naqḍ) and explain the rationale for their order of priority.

Core Idea
The questioner's three strategic moves in debate follow a logical priority: man' first, then mu'āraḍa, then naqḍ.

In classical dialectic (jadal), the questioner (sā'il) has a structured set of options when responding to a proof. This passage outlines three functions—preventing a premise (man'), counter-arguing the claim (mu'āraḍa), and refuting the proof (naqḍ)—and explains why they are ordered as they are. Understanding this order is crucial for conducting a disciplined argument consistent with the adab al-bahth tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three functions
Locate in the Arabic the three terms: man', mu'āraḍa, and naqḍ. Note what each one targets: man' targets a premise, mu'āraḍa targets the claim, naqḍ targets the proof.
2
Understand the priority of man'
The author gives three reasons for giving man' precedence: it is the safest method (free from coercion), it deals with a part of the proof (which naturally precedes the whole), and it is more effective in bringing out the truth. List these reasons in your own words.
3
Differentiate mu'āraḍa vs naqḍ
Mu'āraḍa attacks the claim (mudda'ā) directly, while naqḍ attacks the proof (dalīl). Why is attacking the claim considered stronger? Because the claim is the primary objective; affecting it is more impactful than affecting the proof.
4
Apply the order in a debate scenario
Given a hypothetical claim and its proof, decide which function you would use first, second, and third according to the author's order. This step prepares you for the application task below.
See 'apply_the_tool' for a concrete exercise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان التصديق مقرونا بدليل فللسائل حينئذ ثلاث وظائف: الأولى المنع، والثانية المعارضة، والثالثة النقض. وقدم المنع لأنه أسلم الطرق وعار عن شائبة الغصب، ولأنه متعلق بجزء الدليل، والجزء مقدم على الكل طبعا، ولأنه أدخل في إظهار الصواب. والمعارضة قدمت على النقض لأنها واردة على المدعى وهو المقصود الأصلي، والنقض وارد على الدليل، والدخل في المقصود الأصلي أقوى من الدخل على الدليل. فههنا ثلاث مقالات لبيان الوظائف الثلاث على الترتيب: المقالة الأولى في المنع، والمقالة الثانية في المعارضة، والمقالة الثالثة في النقض.
And if the assent is accompanied by a proof, then the questioner has three functions: first, prevention (al-man'); second, counter-argument (al-mu'araḍa); and third, refutation (al-naqḍ). Prevention is given precedence because it is the safest method and free from any taint of coercion, because it pertains to a part of the proof, and the part naturally precedes the whole, and because it is more effective in bringing out the truth. Counter-argument is given precedence over refutation because it targets the claim itself, which is the primary object, while refutation targets the proof; engagement with the primary object is stronger than engagement with the proof. Therefore, there are three articles to explain the three functions in order: the first article on prevention, the second article on counter-argument, and the third article on refutation.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage explains the three functions of the questioner (sā'il) in dialectical debate when the respondent's claim is supported by a proof. The functions are: man' (preventing a premise), mu'āraḍa (counter-argument), and naqḍ (refutation). The author justifies the order: man' is first because it is safest (avoiding coercion), it deals with a part of the proof (which naturally precedes the whole), and it is more effective in revealing truth. Mu'āraḍa is placed before naqḍ because it directly attacks the claim (the primary objective), whereas naqḍ attacks the proof; affecting the primary objective is stronger. The passage then indicates that three articles will follow, each elaborating one function in order.

Key Takeaway
The questioner's three functions are ordered deliberately: man' is safest and targets part of the proof, mu'āraḍa directly attacks the claim, and naqḍ attacks the proof.

Conditions for Valid Man' (Prevention)
Defines man' and its conditions for validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a prevention (man') is valid by checking the two conditions specified in the text.

Core Idea
A prevention is valid only when the premise is neither self-evident nor already proven.

In jadal, the questioner can object to a premise by demanding proof (man'). But not all premises can be prevented. The author sets clear conditions for when this move is allowed. This lesson walks you through those conditions using the Arabic text and explains why the main claim itself cannot be prevented.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the word *al-man'* in the Arabic. What does the author say it is? It is an action by the questioner on a specific premise.
The definition is: 'the questioner prevents a specific premise from among the premises of the proof'.
2
Extract the two conditions for valid prevention
Look for the phrase 'إذا لم يستدل المعلل عليها ولم تكن تلك المقدمة بديهية جلية'. The two conditions are: (1) the respondent has not brought proof for that premise, and (2) the premise is not self-evident.
If either condition fails, the prevention is invalid (obstinate).
3
Understand the definition of 'muqaddima'
The author defines 'muqaddima' linguistically (that which precedes) and technically (that on which the validity of the proof depends, either as a condition or a part).
This broad definition includes premises and conditions.
4
Distinguish valid from invalid prevention
The text says: 'لا يصح منع المدعى حينئذ' – the claim itself cannot be prevented because prevention is a request for proof, and the proof is already present. The only exception is metaphorical (discussed in next lesson).
This reinforces that man' targets premises, not the final claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الأولى في بيان المنع ومورده. اعلم أن السائل منع مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل إذا لم يستدل المعلل عليها ولم تكن تلك المقدمة بديهية جلية. والمقدمة في اللغة من قدم بمعنى تقدم، وفي العرف ما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل شرطا أو شطرا. ولا يصح منع المدعى حينئذ لأن المنع طلب الدليل والمطلوب حاصل، إلا أن يراد بمنع ذلك المدعى منع شيء من مقدمات دليله، وذا مجاز في النسبة. ويسمى أيضا منعا مجازا عقليا ومجازا حكميا. هذا إذا لم يرد من المدعى المقدمة بعلاقة اللزوم ولم يقدر فوقه شيء. وأما إذا أريدت المقدمة منه أو قدرت فالمدعى مجاز لغوي.
The first article on the explanation of prevention and its subject. Know that the questioner prevents a specific premise from among the premises of the proof if the respondent does not bring proof for it and that premise is not self-evidently clear. The term 'premise' linguistically derives from 'taqaddum' meaning precedence, and in custom it is that upon which the validity of the proof depends, whether as a condition or a part. It is not valid to prevent the claim itself at that time, because prevention is a request for proof, and the requested thing is already present, unless what is meant by preventing that claim is preventing something from among the premises of his proof, and this is a metaphorical attribution. It is also called a rational metaphor and a judgmental metaphor. This is the case if the premise is not intended from the claim by a relationship of implication and nothing is assumed to be above it. But if the premise is intended from it or something is assumed, then the prevention is a linguistic metaphor.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines the first dialectical function, man' (prevention), and its conditions. The author explains that a questioner can only prevent a specific premise of the respondent's proof if two conditions hold: (1) the respondent has not brought a separate proof for that premise, and (2) the premise is not self-evident (badīhī). Otherwise, preventing it would be obstinate (mukābara). The term 'muqaddima' (premise) is defined linguistically as 'that which precedes' and technically as 'that on which the validity of the proof depends, either as a condition or as a constituent part'. Then the author discusses a crucial point: it is not valid to prevent the claim (mad'ā) itself if it is already supported by a proof, because prevention is a request for proof, and the proof is already present. However, there is a metaphorical exception: if what is meant by 'preventing the claim' is actually preventing one of its premises, then this is a metaphorical attribution (majāz fī al-nisba). This is called a rational or judgmental metaphor. The author distinguishes between this and linguistic metaphor: if the premise is not intended via a relationship of implication and nothing is assumed above it, it is a metaphorical attribution; if the premise is directly intended or something is assumed, it becomes a linguistic metaphor.

Key Terms
المنع
المقدمة
بديهية جلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) When the premise is the main claim of the respondent.

Key Takeaway
Man' is a request for proof on a premise, and it is valid only if the premise is not self-evident and not already proven.

Metaphorical Prevention and Its Types
Discusses metaphorical prevention and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between metaphorical prevention (majāz fī al-nisba) and linguistic metaphor (majāz lughawī) when the claim itself is prevented.

Core Idea
Preventing the claim itself is only valid as a metaphorical attribution, and it comes in two types: rational/judgmental and linguistic.

The author previously stated that preventing the claim itself is invalid. But there is an exception: if by 'preventing the claim' one actually means preventing a premise of its proof, that is a metaphorical prevention. This lesson unpacks the two types of metaphor involved and their conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception
Find the phrase 'إلا أن يراد بمنع ذلك المدعى منع شيء من مقدمات دليله'. This introduces the exception: when preventing the claim actually means preventing a premise.
This is called a metaphorical attribution (مجاز في النسبة).
2
Recognize the types of metaphor
The text says it is also called 'مجازا عقليا' (rational metaphor) and 'مجازا حكميا' (judgmental metaphor). These are synonyms for metaphorical attribution.
The metaphor is in the relationship, not the words.
3
Distinguish the two scenarios
The author describes two cases: (1) when the premise is not intended by a relationship of implication (علاقة اللزوم) and nothing is assumed above it – that is metaphorical attribution. (2) when the premise is directly intended or something is assumed above it – that is a linguistic metaphor (مجاز لغوي).
Linguistic metaphor involves transferring the word itself; attributional metaphor involves transferring the action.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Suppose the respondent's claim is 'The world is created' and the proof includes premises. If the questioner says 'I prevent the claim' but means 'I prevent the premise that everything originated has a cause', that is rational metaphor. If he literally means the claim but the claim stands for the premise by linguistic convention, that is linguistic metaphor.
The text provides the tools to classify the type of metaphor.

Checkpoint

Conditions for Valid Objection in Dialectical Debate
Defines conditions for valid objection in dialectical debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for valid objection (manʿ) to a premise and to a claim in a dialectical debate, and distinguish literal from metaphorical objection.

Core Idea
Objection in jadal is a tool for truth, not obstinacy; its validity depends on the epistemic status of the premise and the presence of a proof.

In this passage from the Waldiyya commentary on adab al-bahth, the author clarifies when a questioner can legitimately object to a premise or a claim. Understanding these rules is crucial for engaging in productive theological and juristic debate, where objections must be grounded in proper methodology.

Guided Path
1
Define 'muqaddima' (premise) technically
From the text, a premise is 'that upon which the validity of a proof depends, as a condition or a part.' It includes both conditions and parts of the proof. Note the distinction from linguistic usage.
2
Identify when objection to a premise is valid
Objection to a premise is valid only if (1) the defender has not cited evidence for it, and (2) it is not self-evident. If it is self-evident, objection is obstinacy and inadmissible. If evidence is given, the objection becomes metaphorical.
3
Analyze objection to a claim with proof
The author argues that objecting to a claim that already has a proof is invalid because it requests what is already obtained. This is presented as a conditional argument: if the objection is a request for proof and the proof exists, then the objection is invalid. However, counterarguments are raised: the objector may seek another proof or test the defender.
The author ultimately maintains that proper objection to a proven claim is invalid in the literal sense.
4
Recognize metaphorical uses of objection
When the objector intends to object to a specific premise of the claimant's proof (not the claim itself), this is a metaphor in attribution (majāz fī al-nisba). It is also called intellectual metaphor. If the premise is intended directly, it becomes a linguistic metaphor or ellipsis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السائل منع مقدمة من مقدمات الدليل. والمقدمة في اللغة إما من قدم اللازم أو من قدم التعدي بمعناه أو بمعنى تقدم، فيجوز فيها فتح الدال وكسرها وهو الأشهر. وفي العرف ما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل شرطا أو شطرا، فيتناول الشرائط والأجزاء. وتطلق على ما جعلت جزء قياس أو حجة، وعلى ما يتوقف عليه الشروع في العلم وهو مقدمة العلم، وعلى ما يذكر قبل الشروع في المقاصد لارتباطها به ونفعه فيها وهو مقدمة الكتاب. والمراد هو المعنى الأول. (إذا لم يستدل المعلل عليها) أي على تلك المقدمة بأن لا يورد عليها دليلا. وأما إذا استدل عليها فلا تمنع حقيقة بل مجازا في النسبة أو في الحذف. (ولم تكن) تلك المقدمة (بديهية جلية) وإلا لكان منعها مكابرة وهي غير مسموعة اتفاقا. (ولا يصح منع المدعى حينئذ) أي حين كونه مقرونا بدليل (لأن المنع طلب الدليل والمطلوب حاصل). تقريره: إذا كان المنع طلب الدليل مع كون المطلوب حاصلا كان المانع طالبا التحصيل الحاصل، وكلما كان المانع كذلك كان منع المدعى المدلل غير صحيح. فإذا كان المنع طلب الدليل مع كون المطلوب حاصلا كان منع المدعى المدلل غير صحيح. لكن المقدم حق والتالي مثله. وفيه أنا لا نسلم أن المطلوب حاصل: لم لا يجوز أن يكون المطلوب دليلا آخر للعلم بطرق متعددة؟ وإن سلم فلا تسلم أن منع المدعى المدلل غير صحيح لأنه يجوز أن يكون الطلب للامتحان. اللهم إلا أن يقال المراد لا يصح على الوجه اللائق. وأيضا إنه منقوض بقول الخليل عليه السلام: "ولكن ليطمئن قلبي". (إلا أن يراد) بمنع ذلك المدعى منع شيء من مقدمات دليله (أي مقدمات دليل ذلك المدعى أو تنبيه) وذا (أي منع ذلك المدعى) مجاز في النسبة. ويسمى أيضا منعا مجازا عقليا ومجازا حكميا ومجازا في الإثبات وإسنادا مجازيا. هذا إذا لم يرد من المدعى المقدمة بعلاقة اللزوم ولم يقدر فوقه شيء. وأما إذا أريدت المقدمة منه أو قدرت فالمدعى مجاز لغوي لأنه مستعمل في غير معناه الحقيقي أو حذفي، فحينئذ لا مجاز في النسبة.
The questioner objects to a premise among the premises of the proof. The premise (muqaddima) linguistically is from 'qadama' meaning precedence, so it is permissible to open or break the letter dal, and the latter is more famous. In technical usage, it is that upon which the validity of the proof depends, as a condition or a part, so it includes conditions and parts. It is also applied to what is made a part of a syllogism or argument, and to what the commencement of knowledge depends on (the premise of knowledge), and to what is mentioned before beginning the main topics due to its connection and benefit (the premise of the book). The intended meaning here is the first. (If the defender has not cited evidence for it) i.e., for that premise, by not bringing a proof for it. If he has cited evidence, then it is not objected to literally, but metaphorically in attribution or in ellipsis. (And) that premise (is not self-evident), otherwise objecting to it would be obstinacy, and it is unanimously not heard. (The objection to the claimant is not valid then) i.e., when it is accompanied by a proof (because objection is a request for proof, and the requested is already obtained). Its explanation: if the objection is a request for proof while the requested is obtained, then the objector is requesting something already obtained, and whenever the objector is like that, the objection to the proponent with a proof is invalid. So if the objection is a request for proof while the requested is obtained, then the objection to the proponent with a proof is invalid. But the premise is true and the conclusion is similar. It may be said: we do not concede that the requested is obtained; why may it not be that the requested is another proof for knowledge through multiple paths? And even if conceded, we do not concede that the objection to the proponent with a proof is invalid, because the request may be for testing. Unless it is said that intended is that it is not valid in a proper manner. Also, it is refuted by the saying of al-Khalil (peace be upon him): 'But so that my heart may be assured.' (Unless it is intended) by objection to that claimant is an objection to something from the premises of his proof (i.e., premises of that claimant's proof or a hint), and that (i.e., objection to that claimant) is a metaphor in attribution. It is also called an objection as intellectual metaphor, judgmental metaphor, metaphor in affirmation, and metaphorical attribution. This is if the premise is not intended from the claimant by a relation of necessity and nothing is assumed above it. If the premise is intended from him or assumed, then the claimant is a linguistic metaphor because it is used in other than its literal meaning, or elliptical, then there is no metaphor in attribution.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This is a dialectical passage from the Waldiyya, a commentary on the etiquette of debate. It defines the valid and invalid forms of objection (manʿ) in a dialectical exchange. The author begins by defining the term 'muqaddima' (premise) linguistically and technically. Technically, it is that on which the validity of a proof depends, encompassing conditions and parts. The author clarifies that the intended meaning here is the premise as a part of a proof. Then the conditions for objecting to a premise are given: if the defender has not provided evidence for the premise, the questioner may object to it. If the defender has provided evidence, then objecting to the premise is not valid literally, but may be valid metaphorically in attribution or ellipsis. Additionally, the premise must not be self-evident; objecting to a self-evident premise is obstinacy and universally rejected. Next, the author discusses the objection to the claimant (manʿ al-muddaʿā) when the claimant has provided a proof (dلیل). The objection is invalid because it is a request for proof while the requested is already obtained. This argument is presented in syllogistic form: if the objection is a request for proof and the proof is already present, then the objector is requesting something already available, and such an objection is invalid. However, an objection is then raised: the requested may be another proof for the same claim through different methods, so the proof is not necessarily obtained. Or the request may be for testing. These are rebutted by stating that the intended meaning is that objection in a proper sense is invalid. The author also notes that Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'But so that my heart may be assured' does not invalidate this rule, as his request was for increased certainty, not a rejection of proof. Finally, the author explains that when the objector intends to object to a specific premise of the claimant's proof, then the objection is metaphorical in attribution, known as intellectual metaphor, judgmental metaphor, etc. If the objector intends the premise itself without metaphorical relationship, then it is a linguistic metaphor or ellipsis, and there is no metaphor in attribution.

Permissibility of Metaphorical Objection in Dialectic
Author discusses whether metaphorical objection is allowed against a supported claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a supported claim can be objected to in Islamic dialectic, and apply them to a real theological argument.

Core Idea
Objecting to a supported claim is permissible only by blocking a premise, not by metaphorical objection alone.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle question in dialectic (munazara): can one use metaphorical objection (majāz ʿaqliyy or ḥadhfiyy) against a claim that is supported by evidence? Through a hypothetical dialogue and an example from al-Mawāqif, the author clarifies the proper method: the objector must target a specific premise of the evidence, not merely reject the claim metaphorically. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and equip you to apply this rule in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Read the first line: 'If metaphorical objection is allowed against a supported claim, why is it not allowed here?' Recognize that the author is testing a general principle with a specific case.
2
Understand the author's answer
The author says sound intellect does not forbid it, but such practice is not found in early discourses. This implies that metaphorical objection is not standard; the accepted method is literal premise-blocking.
3
Analyze the example from al-Mawāqif
Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif objected to a proof for the Creator by providing a proof for the opposite (sanad). The author explains that this action shows he did not intend a metaphorical objection but a literal one, because he used a supporting proof.
4
Extract the rule
The passage concludes: for a supported claim, if its evidence is not accepted, one may object by blocking a premise of that evidence. Metaphorical objection alone (global refutation) is not sufficient.

If it is permissible to use metaphorical objection and omission in a claim supported by evidence, then why is it not permissible to use global refutation and metaphorical objection (in the two types: intellectual and omission) against a supported claim? There must be evidence to negate that. I say: sound intellect does not forbid it, but they are not found in their discourses. Yes, objection is directed based on the school of one who makes the opponent of objection the claim, but this is weak and the stronger view is that it is a function of the evidence. We have seen some great scholar prevent a claim supported by evidence (with a non-accepted evidence) with a supporting proof, meaning a proof that supports the opposite of that supported claim. If he meant metaphor in attribution and returning it to something from the premises of his evidence, he would not have mentioned a proof that supports the opposite of the claim—this is clear. Likewise, if he meant metaphor in omission, he would not have mentioned a proof that supports the opposite of the claim. First: with a fatḥa on the wāw and shadda, the opposite of the other; its aspect is not hidden. Then he prevented a specific premise from the premises of his evidence, meaning the claimant or the one supporting. The intent is some great scholar: Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif, where he said in the path of some later scholars in proving the Creator: 'All contingent things collectively are possible, so they have a cause, and it is not within that collection since the cause precedes the effect, nor is it a part of it since the cause of the whole is a cause of every part.' And it was objected: 'If by 'cause' in your statement 'they have a cause' you mean the complete cause, then why is it not permissible that it be the collection itself? And your statement 'since the cause precedes the effect' is denied in the complete cause.' Up to the end of what he said. His statement: 'Then why is it not permissible'—its exposition: your statement 'and it is not the same as that collection' is denied, not accepted. So the result of preventing the claim is requesting an accepted proof, since the mentioned proof is not accepted by the objector. Thus Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif's statement 'and your statement since the cause etc.' is an answer to an anticipated question. The exposition of the question from the side of the one supporting: 'How do you prevent this claim and demand a proof for it while he has mentioned a proof for it?' The exposition of the answer: 'What I mean by my prevention is requesting an accepted proof, and your mentioned proof is not accepted because some of its premises are prevented.' Thus it is known from this that for a claim supported by evidence, if its evidence is not accepted, it is permissible to prevent it, provided that he prevents a premise from the premises of his evidence—the implication—and he was not able to do that. But if you intended or estimated, then the claim is a linguistic metaphor, so there is no metaphor in attribution here. And we have seen some great scholar prevent a claim supported by evidence with a proof first, or prevent a specific premise from the premises of his evidence second. He said in the gloss: He is Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif, where he said in the path of some later scholars in proving the Creator: 'All contingent things collectively are possible, so they have a cause, and it is not the same as that collection since the cause precedes the effect, nor is it a part of it since the cause of the whole is a cause of every part.' And it was objected: 'If by 'cause' in his statement 'they have a cause' he means the complete cause, then why is it not permissible that it be the collection itself? And your statement 'since the cause precedes the effect' is denied in the complete cause.' Up to the end of what he said. His statement: 'Then why is it not permissible'—its exposition: your statement 'and it is not the same as that collection' is denied, not accepted. The result of preventing the claim then is requesting an accepted proof, since the mentioned proof is not accepted by the objector. Thus Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif's statement 'and your statement since the cause etc.' is an answer to an anticipated question. The exposition of the question from the side of the opponent: 'How do you prevent this claim and demand a proof for it while he has mentioned a proof for it?' The exposition of the answer: 'What is meant by my prevention is requesting an accepted proof, and your mentioned proof is not accepted because some of its premises are prevented.' End. So it is known from this that the claimant, if his evidence is not accepted, may be prevented, but on condition that he prevents a premise from the premises of his evidence?

Commentary
This passage discusses a dialectical issue: whether it is permissible to use metaphorical objection against a claim that is supported by evidence. The author frames the question as a hypothetical objection: if metaphorical objection (both intellectual and omission-based) is allowed against supported claims in general, why is it not allowed in the specific case of a supported claim? The answer is that sound intellect does not forbid it, but such practice is not found in the discourses of the early scholars. However, the objection is directed according to the school that considers the opponent of the objection to be the claim itself, but this is a weaker view; the stronger view is that objection is a function of the evidence. The author then cites an example from a great scholar (Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif) who prevented a claim supported by evidence by providing a proof that supports the opposite of the claim. The author explains that if the scholar had intended a metaphorical interpretation of the attribution, he would not have needed to provide a supporting proof; thus his act shows he meant a literal prevention. The example involves a proof for the existence of the Creator: all contingent things require a cause, which cannot be part of the collection. The objection is that if 'cause' means the complete cause, then it could be the collection itself, and the statement that cause precedes effect is not necessary for a complete cause. The response is that the claim is not accepted because some premises are prevented. The lesson concludes that a supported claim can be objected to only by blocking a premise of its evidence, not by metaphorical means alone.

Divisions of Objection and Definition of Supporting Proof
Author classifies objection into two types and defines the supporting proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an objection in a dialectical debate as bare of a supporting proof or accompanied by one, and evaluate whether a supporting proof meets the definitional criteria.

Core Idea
Objections in dialectic are useless without a supporting proof that, if possible, overturns the opponent's claim.

In Islamic dialectic (munazara), when a claim is made, the opponent can object. The objection can be simply 'I don't accept that' without evidence, or it can be backed by a proof. The author here clarifies these two types and defines what counts as a supporting proof (sanad). Understanding this distinction is crucial for following the rules of debate and constructing valid objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of objection
Find in the text the division of objection (man') into two types: bare (mujarrad 'an al-sanad) and accompanied (maqrūn bihī). Note that the objection itself can be real or metaphorical, but the division applies to all cases.
2
Understand the definition of the supporting proof (sanad)
Locate the definition: the sanad is what the objector mentions based on his belief that it necessitates the opposite of the objected proposition. The author emphasizes that the sanad does not need to be actually true; only intellectual possibility suffices.
3
Examine the boundaries of the definition
The author notes that a more general or more specific sanad does not invalidate the definition; only a different (mubāyin) sanad does. Also, the sanad is considered a conceptualization (taṣawwur), not necessarily an assent (taṣdīq).
4
Distinguish the two ways the sanad can be presented
The sanad can be presented on the basis of possibility (tajwīz) or on the basis of certainty (qaṭ'). In the possibility case, the objector offers a possible alternative equal to the opposite; in the certainty case, he asserts a fact directly contradicting the opponent's claim.
The example with the apparition (shabah) illustrates both: 'Why is it not allowed that it be rational?' (possibility) vs 'How do you say it is not a human while it is rational?' (certainty).

Chapter: On explaining the divisions of objection. The objection, absolutely whether it is real or metaphorical, is divided into two types: because it is either an objection bare of a supporting proof (i.e., devoid of it) or accompanied by it. And the supporting proof: he made it apparent to indicate the difference, because the first definition is for the essence and the second division is for individuals as is well known, and the statement 'repeating a thing as a definition intends the same as the first' is a separator often deviated from. It is what the objector mentions based on his assumption, i.e., his assumption and belief, that it necessitates the opposite of the objected proposition. And the definition is not invalidated by the more general supporting proof, absolutely or from one aspect, but by the different because it necessitates the opposite in the objector's view. The supporting proof is defined here because he intended to prepare for the subsequent discussion, so it cannot be objected that it is a repetition without benefit. It is sufficient in using it (i.e., the supporting proof, referring to the second supporting proof by usage or to the first) that it be possible intellectually, i.e., in the mind or intellectual possibility, so it is either an adverb or an absolute object. As for the one who made it a distinction from possibility, he neglects the discussion of distinction, so understand. And it is not necessary that it be actualized in reality, and there is an indication that the supporting proof is of the category of conceptualizations, although some allowed it to be of the category of assents. It may be mentioned on the basis of possibility, as you say: 'This apparition is not a human, and everything not a human is not laughing.' So the objector intended to deny the minor premise with the supporting proof which is mentioned on the basis of possibility. And to this he alluded by his saying: 'It is said: we do not accept that it (i.e., this apparition) is not a human; why is it not allowed that this apparition be rational?' This is a supporting proof equal to the opposite of the objected proposition. And analogize to it the more specific and the more general, absolutely or from one aspect. And it may be mentioned on the basis of certainty, i.e., the mind's certainty and decisiveness, as it is said: 'We do not accept that this apparition is not a human. How do you say it is not a human while it is rational?' Or it is said: 'We do not accept that it is not a human. Your statement is only valid if that apparition were not rational, but it is not so.' i.e., not rational.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a chapter that explains the divisions of objection (man') in dialectic. The author states that objection, whether real (metaphorical) or literal, is divided into two types: bare (without a supporting proof) and accompanied by a supporting proof (sanad). The supporting proof is defined as what the objector mentions based on his belief that it necessitates the opposite of the objected proposition. The definition is not invalidated by a more general supporting proof, but only by a different one. The author then notes that the supporting proof is defined here as a prelude to the next discussion, so it is not a repetition. It is sufficient for the supporting proof to be intellectually possible; actual truth is not required. This indicates that the supporting proof falls under conceptualizations, though some consider it under assents. The supporting proof can be presented on the basis of possibility (e.g., 'Why is it not allowed that this apparition be rational?') or on the basis of certainty (e.g., 'How do you say it is not a human while it is rational?'). In the possibility case, the objector gives a possible alternative that is equal to the opposite of the denied proposition. In the certainty case, the objector asserts a fact that directly contradicts the opponent's claim. The author then repeats the classification with slight variation, emphasizing that the supporting proof is what the objector mentions believing it necessitates the opposite, and that it is sufficient that the objector does not consider it false, not that he believes it true. The examples use a ghost/shape (shabah) and the attribute of being rational (natiq) to illustrate.

The supporting proof in dialectic only needs to be intellectually possible, not necessarily true, for the objection to be considered valid.
