Bare vs. Grounded Objections in Jadal
Author divides objection into bare and grounded, defines the ground, and its two modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an objection in a jadal debate is bare or grounded, and if grounded, whether the ground is presented as a possibility (tajwiz) or a certainty (qat').

Core Idea
In jadal, an objection without a ground is empty; a ground can be merely possible or decisively certain.

In dialectical debate (jadal), objecting to an opponent's claim is a key move. However, not all objections are equal: some are supported by a ground (sanad), while others are not. This passage from the Waladiyya teaches you to distinguish between these two types and further classify grounded objections by the strength of the ground's assertion. Mastering this classification is essential for conducting structured and fair debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (man')
Locate the statement where the objector denies or challenges a claim. Is it a simple denial or does it include a reason?
2
Check for a ground (sanad)
Determine if the objection includes a basis that the objector claims entails the opposite of the objected proposition. If no basis is given, it is bare (mujarrad); if a basis is provided, it is grounded (muqaran).
3
Determine the mode of the ground
If grounded, decide whether the ground is presented as a mere possibility (tajwiz) — e.g., 'maybe it is not' — or as a certainty (qat') — using definitive language like 'how can you say... it is not so?'
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take a practice scenario from Islamic jurisprudence or theology and classify the objection according to the above steps.
For instance, in a debate about qiyas, an objection might be: 'We do not concede that qiyas is valid.' This is bare. 'We do not concede because qiyas leads to uncertainty, and uncertainty cannot ground obligation.' This is grounded with a qat' mode.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان أقسام المنع. المنع إما مجرد عن السند أو مقرون به. والسند ما ذكره المانع لزعمه أنه يستلزم نقيض الممنوع. ويكفي في الاستناد به جوازه عقلا. فقد يذكر السند على سبيل التجويز كما تقول: هذا الشبح ليس بإنسان وكل ما ليس بإنسان ليس بضاحك. وقد يذكر على سبيل القطع كأن يقال: لا نسلم أن هذا الشبح ليس بإنسان كيف تقول إنه ليس بإنسان وهو ناطق؟ أو يقال: إنما يصح ما ذكرته لو كان غير ناطق وليس كذلك.
Chapter on explaining the divisions of objection. The objection is either bare of the ground or coupled with it. The ground is what the objector mentions in his claim that it entails the opposite of the objected-to proposition. It suffices for relying on it that it be intellectually possible. So the ground may be mentioned as a possibility, as when you say: This apparition is not a human, and everything that is not a human is not laughing. And it may be mentioned as a certainty, as when one says: We do not concede that this apparition is not a human; how can you say it is not a human while it is speaking? Or one says: What you mentioned would be correct only if it were not speaking, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of objection (manʿ) in dialectic: one is bare (mujarrad) of any supporting ground (sanad), and the other is accompanied by a ground. The ground is the reason or basis that the objector believes necessitates the opposite of the proposition being objected to. The author emphasizes that for the ground to be valid, it is enough that it is intellectually possible—it does not need to be actually true. The ground is then further divided into two modes of presentation: (1) as a possibility (tajwīz), where the objector merely suggests an alternative possibility, as in the example: 'This apparition is not a human, and everything not human is not laughing'; (2) as a certainty (qaṭʿ), where the objector asserts the ground decisively, as in the example: 'We do not concede that this apparition is not a human – how can you say it is not a human while it is speaking?' or by explicitly pointing out the mistake. This classification is fundamental in jadal (dialectical debate) for correctly framing objections and responses.

المنع
السند
التجويز
القطع

A) possibility (tajwiz)
B) certainty (qat')
C) bare objection

Key Takeaway
An objection in jadal is either bare or grounded; a grounded objection can present its ground as a mere possibility or as a certainty, and intellectual possibility suffices for its validity.

The Solution-Objection and Three Sources of Error
Author defines the solution-type objection and lists three sources of logical error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three sources of error in an opponent's argument and craft a solution-objection that explains the source.

Core Idea
A solution-objection not only denies but unties the knot of confusion.

In dialectical debate, not every objection is a simple denial. The most powerful objections expose the very source of the opponent's error. This lesson introduces the 'solution' (ḥall) as a tool to dissect what went wrong, giving you a sharper edge in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish simple denial from solution
A simple denial (manʿ) only rejects a premise. A solution (ḥall) additionally reveals why the opponent erred, by pinpointing the source of error.
2
Identify the three sources of error
The author lists: (1) confusion of one denotation with another, (2) confusion of one concept with another, (3) illusion that something occurs that would make the argument valid.
Each source corresponds to a distinct logical mistake.
3
Examine the example for confusion of concept
The second example: 'The possibility of the possible is not non-existent; otherwise possibility would be negated.' The solution spots a distinction between 'its possibility is not' and 'its possibility is not for it'.
The error is conflating two similar but distinct concepts.
4
Practice applying the solution
Given a problematic argument, determine which source of error is present and craft a ḥall that denies the implication while explaining the confusion.
Use the examples in the passage as templates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسمى المنع الذي سنده في الصورة الثالثة حلا، لأن فيه بيان منشأ الغلط. والحل هو بيان منشأ الغلط. ومنشأ الغلط ثلاثة: الأول اشتباه مدلول بآخر كما في الكتاب. الثاني اشتباه مفهوم بآخر كما إذا قيل: إمكان الممكن ليس معدوما في الخارج وإلا لا نتفى الإمكان على تقدير ثبوته، والتالي باطل، وحله أن يمنع الملازمة مستندا بأنها إنما تصح لو لم يكن بين إمكانه لا وإمكانه له فرق، لكن بينهما فرق. الثالث توهم وقوع شيء يتم ما ذكره على تقدير وقوعه كما إذا قيل: الشيء الذي يستلزم وجوده وعدمه المطلوب إما موجود أو معدوم، وأيا ما كان يتحقق المطلوب. وهذه المغالطة من العامة، وحلها أن يمنع الملازمة.
And the objection whose ground is in the third form is called a solution (ḥall), because it contains a clarification of the source of error. The solution is the clarification of the source of error. The sources of error are three: first, confusion of one denotation with another, as in the Book. Second, confusion of one concept with another, as when it is said: The possibility of the possible is not non-existent in the external world; otherwise possibility would be negated on the assumption of its existence, and the consequent is false. Its solution is to deny the implication with a ground that it would be valid only if there were no distinction between 'its possibility is not' and 'its possibility is not for it', but there is a distinction. Third, the illusion of something occurring such that what is mentioned would be completed on the assumption of its occurrence, as when it is said: The thing whose existence and non-existence entail the required is either existent or non-existent, and whichever it is, the required is realized. This fallacy is among the common ones, and its solution is to deny the implication.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage introduces a special kind of objection called al-ḥall (the solution), which is an objection that includes not only a ground (sanad) but also an explanation of the source of error (manashaʾ al-ghalaṭ) in the opponent's argument. The author states that such an objection is called a 'solution' because it unties the knot of confusion. Three sources of error are then listed and exemplified: (1) Confusion of one denotation (madlūl) with another, as occurs in the study of logic texts (al- Kitāb) where different meanings of a term are mixed. (2) Confusion of one concept (mafhūm) with another, illustrated by a fallacy about the possibility of the possible. The solution involves pointing out that the implication is invalid because there is a distinction between the two concepts. (3) Illusion that something will occur, such that the argument would be valid only if that occurrence happened, but it doesn't. An example is given: 'The thing whose existence and non-existence both entail the required is either existent or non-existent; whichever, the required is realized.' This is a common fallacy, solved by denying the implication with the ground that the situation presupposes the thing's existence in a way that is not actually the case. The passage thus provides a key tool for dialectical refutation.

Key Terms
الحل
منشأ الغلط
اشتباه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Confusion of denotation
B) Confusion of concept
C) Illusion of occurrence
D) Denial of premise

Key Takeaway
A solution-objection clarifies the source of error, making it more powerful than a simple denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine, one argues: 'Nabidh is intoxicating like wine, so it is forbidden.' A solution-objection could point out confusion between two types of intoxication. Identify the source of error and propose a ḥall.
?
Consider the argument: 'If the world had no creator, it would be eternal; but it is not eternal, so it has a creator.' Which source of error might be involved? Draft a solution-objection.

Five Scholarly Opinions on al-Hall
Author lists five different scholarly positions on the nature and application of al-hall in dialectics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the five scholarly opinions on al-hall and identify the key differences in their scope and usage.

Core Idea
Al-hall has multiple meanings; knowing them sharpens your dialectical analysis.

In this passage, the author enumerates five distinct positions on the meaning of al-hall in dialectical debate. Each opinion reflects a different understanding of how 'solution' relates to refutation and objection. Mastering these nuances is essential for reading later works on jadal and munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five opinions
Read the Arabic passage and list the five opinions in order. Note the scholar or source attributed to each (e.g., 'commentator of Hikmat al-'Ayn', al-Taftazani, etc.).
The first opinion is attributed to 'some', the second to the commentator of Hikmat al-'Ayn, third to al-Taftazani, fourth to Muslih al-Din al-Lari, fifth to al-Sinan al-Kanji.
2
Extract the core definition for each
For each opinion, write one sentence capturing how it defines or applies al-hall. Focus on scope (who uses it, when it occurs) and relation to naqd and man'.
Opinion 5: al-hall is a declaration of error without demanding proof, contrasted with man'.
3
Compare pairwise similarities and differences
Choose two opinions and list one similarity and one difference. For example, opinion 2 and 4 both involve naqd, but opinion 4 restricts hall to after naqd.
Use the three key terms: al-hall, al-naqd al-ijmali, al-man'.
4
Relate to dialectical practice
Consider a hypothetical debate: a mustadill presents a proof, and a sa'il objects. Which opinion would best describe the response if the sa'il says 'this is an error, and here's why'?
Opinion 5 matches that scenario: hall locates the error without demanding proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل وبالجملة في الحمل خمسة أقوال: الأول عموم الحل للمستدل والسائل كما ذهب إليه البعض: والثاني إطلاق الحل على النقض الإجمالي كما هو المستفاد من كلام شارح حكمة العين. والثالث للمباينة بين الحل والنقض والمنع كما هو المستفاد من كلام التفتازاني في مواضع من التلويح. الرابع ما ذهب إليه مصلح الدين اللاري من أن الحل لا يقع إلا بعد النقض الإجمالي. والخامس ما ذهب إليه السنان الكنجي، حيث قال إن الحل شاع وكثر استعماله عند الأصوليين في تعيين موضع الغلط وهو وإن كان نوعا من المنع لكن لنوع خصوصية قد يذكر في مقابلة المنع، ولا يقصد بالحل طلب الدليل كما هو الظاهر من المنع بل يقصد أن ما ذكرته غلط ومنشؤه فهم ذا من كذا وليس الأمر كذلك ولولا ذلك لما وقعت في الغلط.
It was said: In summary, there are five opinions regarding al-hall: The first is the generality of al-hall to both the one who provides proof and the questioner, as some held. The second is applying al-hall to the general refutation, as understood from the words of the commentator of Hikmat al-'Ayn. The third is the distinction between al-hall, al-naqd, and al-man', as understood from the words of al-Taftazani in places of al-Talwih. The fourth is what was held by Muslih al-Din al-Lari: that al-hall only occurs after a general refutation. The fifth is what was held by al-Sinan al-Kanji, who said that al-hall became widespread and frequently used among the usuliyyun to designate the location of error, and even though it is a type of man', due to a special characteristic it may be mentioned in contrast to man', and the intent of al-hall is not to demand proof as is apparent from man', but rather the intent is that what you mentioned is an error and its source is understanding this from that, and it is not so, and were it not for that, you would not have fallen into error.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage enumerates five distinct scholarly views on the concept of 'al-hall' (the solution) in dialectical debate. The first opinion considers that al-hall applies equally to both the disputant presenting the proof (al-mustadill) and the questioner (al-sa'il). The second opinion restricts al-hall to the meaning of 'general refutation' (al-naqd al-ijmali), as inferred from the commentary on Hikmat al-'Ayn. The third opinion, attributed to al-Taftazani, maintains a clear distinction between al-hall, al-naqd (refutation), and al-man' (prevention). The fourth opinion, from Muslih al-Din al-Lari, holds that al-hall can only occur after a general refutation has been made. The fifth opinion, from al-Sinan al-Kanji, notes that among the scholars of usul al-fiqh, al-hall has become the common term for pinpointing the location of the error. Although it is a type of man', it is often contrasted with man' because its purpose is not to demand proof but to declare that the opponent's statement is erroneous and to explain why.

Key Terms
الحل
النقض الإجمالي
المنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fifth opinion (al-Sinan al-Kanji), what distinguishes al-hall from al-man'?
A) Al-hall demands proof, while al-man' does not.
B) Al-hall is a declaration of error without demanding proof, while al-man' demands proof.
C) Al-hall is only used by usuliyyun, while al-man' is used by logicians.
D) Al-hall always occurs after naqd, while al-man' occurs before.

Key Takeaway
The five opinions show that 'al-hall' is a contested term in dialectics; its meaning shifts from general application to specific refutation to error designation.

Obligation of the Mu'allil When the Sa'il Objects
Author defines the duty of the mu'allil when the sa'il raises a prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the duties of the mu'allil when the sa'il objects and apply them to a dialectical scenario.

Core Idea
Prove what the sa'il objects to—by proof, clarification, or doctrinal explanation.

In the dialectical tradition (adab al-bahth), the mu'allil (proof-provider) must respond appropriately when the sa'il (objector) raises a prevention (man'). This lesson unpacks the specific duties outlined by the author, showing how to handle objections systematically.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context: mu'allil vs sa'il
The mu'allil is defending a claim; the sa'il objects by preventing acceptance of a premise. Recognize the roles.
2
Identify what the sa'il objects to
The object can be an unproven claim (muda'a) or an unproven premise (muqaddima) of the proof. If the premise is itself proven, it becomes a claim in its own right.
The text emphasizes 'being unproven' as the condition.
3
Learn the four methods of proof
The mu'allil can: (a) provide a proof and invalidate the support that entails the opposite of the prevented part, (b) clarify the intended meaning of the prevented part to nullify the objection, (c) explain the school of thought underlying the prevented part, or (d) clarify the components of the claim or proof when the prevention is an approximation.
These methods are alternatives or complementary.
4
Apply the rule: every directed demand must be fulfilled
The objection is a valid demand ('طلب موجه'), so the mu'allil must prove what was objected to. Failure to do so leaves the objection standing.
This is the core duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان وظيفة المعلل عند منع السائل. الواجب على المعلل عند منع السائل مدعاه الغير المدلل أو مقدمة دليله من حيث هي هي بأن تكون غير مدللة، إذ المقدمة المدللة تكون دعوى من حيث هي مدللة. إثبات ما منعه السائل، وقد يعم ذكر الدليل وإبطال السند اللازم لنقيض الممنوع فقط، وتحرير المراد من أجزاء الممنوع بحيث يسقط المنع، وبيان المذهب الذي بني عليه الممنوع كذلك، وتحرير أجزاء المدعى أو الدليل كذلك عند كون الممنوع تقريبا. لأن هذا الإثبات مطلوب المانع طلبا موجها، وكل مطلوب شأنه كذا واجب على المعلل.
Section on explaining the function of the mu'allil when the sa'il objects. The duty of the mu'allil when the sa'il objects to his claim—which is not proven—or to a premise of his proof, in itself, that is, as being unproven, because a proven premise is a claim in itself as proven, is to prove what the sa'il has objected to. This proof may include mentioning the proof and invalidating the support that necessarily opposes the prevented part only, clarifying the intended meaning of the parts of the prevented part such that the prevention is removed, explaining the school of thought upon which the prevented part is built likewise, and clarifying the parts of the claim or the proof likewise when the prevented part is an approximation. Because this proof is demanded by the objector in a directed demand, and every demand whose nature is like that is obligatory upon the mu'allil.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage begins a new section detailing the responsibilities of the mu'allil (the one who provides the proof) when the sa'il (the questioner or objector) raises a prevention (man'). The author states that the mu'allil is obligated to prove whatever the sa'il objects to, whether it is an unproven claim (muda'a) or an unproven premise of the proof. The emphasis is that if the premise is itself proven, then it becomes a claim in its own right. The proof (ithbat) can take several forms: (1) providing a direct proof (dalil) and invalidating the support that entails the opposite of the prevented part, (2) clarifying the intended meaning of the parts of the prevented part to nullify the objection, (3) explaining the underlying school (madhhab) on which the prevented part is based, and (4) clarifying the components of the claim or proof when the prevented part is an approximation (taqrib). The reasoning is that the objection is a valid demand, and any such demand makes the proof obligatory on the mu'allil.

The mu'allil is obligated to prove what the sa'il objects to, and can do so through proof, clarification, or doctrinal explanation.

Five Opinions on the Nature of Hall in Dialectic
Discussion of five scholarly positions on hall in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five scholarly positions on the definition and application of hall (solution) in dialectical argumentation.

Core Idea
Hall is not just any objection—it is the surgical identification of the error's source.

This passage delves into a nuanced debate among scholars about what exactly constitutes a 'hall' (solution) in a dialectical exchange. Understanding these five opinions is crucial for mastering the art of munazara, as it clarifies the precise tools available to a disputant after a refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common thread
All five opinions agree that hall is a type of objection (man'), but they differ on its scope, timing, and relationship to total refutation (naqd ijmaili).
2
Extract each opinion
For each of the five, note the scholar (if named) and the key claim about hall: (1) general for arguer and questioner; (2) equated with total refutation; (3) distinct from naqd and man'; (4) only after total refutation; (5) used to pinpoint error, a type of man' with specific purpose.
The opinions are numbered in the passage.
3
Compare the opinions
Which equate hall with total refutation? Which restrict its timing? Which emphasize its diagnostic function? Contrast al-Lari's position with al-Kanji's.
4
Apply the definition to a scenario
Imagine a dialectical exchange: after a total refutation, the opponent says 'Your argument fails because you confused the effective cause.' Which opinion(s) would classify this move as a hall?
Consider al-Lari's restriction and al-Kanji's wider usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الملازمة مستندا بأنها إنما تتم لو كان عدم ذلك الشيء مع بقاء صفة الاستلزام وليس كذلك فان عدمه لعدم ذاته وصفاته معا أو لعدم صفته فقط ، وربما يقال الحل على المنع الذى سنده على سبيل التجويز وبين منشأ الغلط وهى المنع الذي ورد على عدم الفرق بين الشيئين كما إذا قيل لو لم يكن بين إمكانه لا ولا إمكانه له فرق لا نتفى الإمكان على تقدير ثبوته، لكن المقدم حق والتالى مثله ومنع المقدمة الاستثنائية فان منعها يسمى حلا، سواء كان مجردا أو مع السند وبين منشأ الغلط أولا . قيل وبالجملة في الحمل خمسة أقوال : الأول عموم الحل للمستدل والسائل كما ذهب إليه البعض : والثاني إطلاق الحل على النقض الإجمالي كما هو المستفاد من كلام شارح حكمة العين. والثالث للمباينة بين الحل والنقض والمنع كما هو المستفاد من كلام التفتازانى فى مواضع من التلويح . الرابع ماذهب إليه مصلح الدين اللارى من أن الحل لا يقع إلا بعد النقض الإجمالي . والخامس ما ذهب إليه السنان الكنجي، حيث قال إن الحل شاع وكثر استعماله عند الأصوليين في تعيين موضع الغلط وهو و إن كان نوعا من المنع لكن لنوع خصوصية قد يذكر في مقابلة المنع ، ولا يقصد بالحل طلب الدليل كما هو الظاهر من المنع بل يقصد أن ما ذكرته غلط ومنشؤه فهم ذا من كذا وليس الأمر كذلك ولولا ذلك لما وقعت فى الغلط ) وأكثر وقوع الحل بعد النقض الاجمالي ) وفيه رد على من حصر وقوع الحل بعد النقض الإجمالى كما يقال : لا نسلم جريان هذا الدليل أو تخلف حكمه وإنما يجرى أو إنما يتخلف لولم يكن بين ذا وهذا فرق لكن بينها فرق ، وكأنه يقول الناقض إنما يصح دليلك لو كانت هذه المقدمة كذا ) وستعرف النقض الإجمالى ) فى المقالة الثانية إن شاء الله تعالى .
The entailment is based on the fact that it is only completed if the absence of that thing occurs while the attribute of implication remains, and it is not so, for its absence is due to the absence of its essence and attributes together, or the absence of its attribute only. And perhaps it is said: The solution (hall) to the objection (man') whose support (sanad) is on the basis of possibility, and he explains the source of error. And it is the objection that is raised against the lack of difference between two things, as when it is said: If there were no difference between its possibility and its non-possibility, then possibility would be negated upon the assumption of its existence. But the antecedent is true and the consequent is similar to it, and he objects to the conditional premise, for objecting to it is called hall, whether it is bare or with the sanad, and he explains the source of error first. It was said: In summary, there are five opinions concerning hall: First: The generality of hall for the arguer and the questioner, as some held. Second: The application of hall to total refutation (naqd ijmaili), as understood from the commentary of Hikmat al-Ayn. Third: The distinction between hall, refutation, and objection, as understood from al-Taftazani in places in al-Talwih. Fourth: What Muslih al-Din al-Lari held, that hall only occurs after total refutation. Fifth: What al-Sinan al-Kanji held, that hall has become widespread and much used among usuliyyun in identifying the place of error, and although it is a type of objection, due to a certain specificity it may be mentioned in contrast to objection; and by hall one does not intend to demand the proof as is apparent from objection, but rather one intends that what you mentioned is an error and its source is such and such understanding, and it is not so, and if it were not for that you would not have fallen into error. And most occurrence of hall is after total refutation. And in it there is a refutation of one who restricts the occurrence of hall to after total refutation, as it is said: We do not concede the running of this proof or the falling away of its ruling; it only runs or only falls away if there were no difference between this and that, but there is a difference between them. And it is as if the objector (naqid) says: Your proof is only valid if this premise were such. You will know total refutation in the second article, God willing.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of 'hall' (solution) in dialectical argumentation. It begins by explaining the foundation of entailment (luzum) and then introduces the idea that hall is a response to an objection (man') that has a support (sanad). The author explains that hall identifies the source of error, and gives an example: if there were no difference between possibility and its opposite, then possibility would be negated. The objection to the conditional premise is called hall. Then the author presents five scholarly opinions on hall: (1) Some say hall is general for both the arguer and the questioner. (2) Some apply the term 'hall' to total refutation (naqd ijmaili). (3) Al-Taftazani distinguishes between hall, refutation (naqd), and objection (man'). (4) Muslih al-Din al-Lari says hall only occurs after total refutation. (5) Al-Sinan al-Kanji says hall is used by usuliyyun to pinpoint the error, and it is a type of objection but with a specific purpose—not to demand proof but to show the source of error. The passage notes that most occurrences of hall are after total refutation, and it refutes those who restrict it to that position. An example is given: 'We do not concede the application of this proof... because there is a difference.' The author promises to explain total refutation in the second article. This passage clarifies the terminology and positions in jadal methodology.

The Mu'allil's Obligation and Direct Proof
Explains the mu'allil's obligation and introduces direct proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mu'allil's obligation when the sa'il objects and describe the method of direct proof (ithbat bi al-dhat).

Core Idea
The mu'allil must prove the objected claim; direct proof establishes it without invalidating the support.

In dialectical debate, when the questioner (sa'il) objects to a claim or premise, the arguer (mu'allil) must respond by proving what was objected to. This lesson explains the nature of that obligation and introduces direct proof, which involves adducing evidence or clarifying the doctrine without the intermediate step of refuting the objector's support.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mu'allil's obligation
Locate in the passage the exact duty of the mu'allil when the sa'il objects. The text states that the mu'allil must prove what the sa'il objected to (ithbat ma mana'ahu).
This is the central requirement.
2
Understand the reason for the obligation
The passage argues that the proof is obligatory because it is demanded by the objector as a directed demand (talab muwajjah), and every such demand is incumbent upon the mu'allil.
The obligation is both customary and religious.
3
Recognize the forms of proof
The mu'allil can fulfil the obligation in several ways: mentioning evidence, invalidating the support, clarifying the intended meaning, explaining the underlying doctrine, etc.
These are general methods.
4
Focus on direct proof
Direct proof (ithbat bi al-dhat) is defined as proof without the intermediate step of invalidating the support. It involves presenting a proof (real or effective) that yields the very prevented thing or its equivalent.
Direct proof is the more straightforward method.

مساواة السند للمنع وأخصيته منه مساواته لنقيض الممنوع وأخصيته منه ) إذ المشهور أن النسب في السند تعتبر بالقياس إلى نقيض الممنوع ، يعنى أن قولهم هذا السند مساو للمنع وأخص منه مجاز فى النسبة ، والمراد مسا ولنقيض الممنوع أو أخص منه الملابسة بين المنع وبين تلك المساواة والأخصية ، إذ المنع كأنه مكان لما قاله في التقرير فاعرفه ، ولا يبعد أن يقال المراد من المنع هنا نقيض الممنوع بمناسبة اللزوم بينهما ، فذا
Section: On explaining the duty of the mu'allil (arguer) when the sa'il (questioner) objects. (What is incumbent upon the mu'allil) by customary obligation, nay by religious obligation—so reflect—when the sa'il objects to his claim (i.e., the claim of the mu'allil absolutely, whether theoretical or intuitive) that is not proven, or to a premise of his proof as such (i.e., that it is not proven), is to prove what the sa'il objected to. And that proof (ithbat) is general, whether by mentioning the evidence, invalidating the support (sanad) that is equal to the opposite of the prevented thing (al-mamnu'), clarifying the intended meaning of the parts of the prevented thing such that the objection falls, explaining the doctrine upon which the prevented thing is based, and clarifying the parts of the claim or the proof when the prevented thing is an approximation. Because this proof is demanded by the objector (al-mani') as a directed demand, and every matter that is such is incumbent upon the mu'allil. It follows that this proof is incumbent upon the mu'allil. Then it reflects what is demanded. And most occurrence of hall is after total refutation, as it is said: We do not concede the application of this proof or its rule differs; it only applies or only fails if there were no difference between this and that, but there is a difference between them. And the objector (naqid) says: Your proof is only valid if this premise were such. You will know total refutation in the second article, God willing. Section: On explaining the duty of the mu'allil when the sa'il objects. (What is incumbent upon the mu'allil when the sa'il objects to his claim that is not proven or to a premise of his proof as such, that is, it being not proven—for a proven premise is a claim as proven) is to prove what the sa'il objected to. And it may include mentioning the evidence and invalidating the support that is necessary for the opposite of the prevented thing only, clarifying the intended meaning of the parts of the prevented thing such that the objection falls, and explaining the doctrine upon which the prevented thing is based similarly, and clarifying the parts of the claim or the proof similarly when the prevented thing is an approximation. Because this proof is demanded by the objector as a directed demand, and every matter that is such is incumbent upon the mu'allil. And that proof is of two types: One is proof by essence (ithbat bi al-dhat), i.e., without the intermediate step of invalidating the support, and it is (mentioning a proof, really or effectively, worthwhile for clarification and explaining the doctrine, (yields) the very (prevented thing) or what is equal to it or more specific than it absolutely, if the approximation exists in each. And the other is proof by mediation (ithbat bi al-wasita), which is (invalidating the support that is equal to the objection, i.e., equal to the opposite of the prevented thing. And it is possible that the attribution of equality to the objection is a mental metaphor. And likewise invalidating the support that is absolutely more general than it in reality, which is more general in one respect than the prevented thing itself because if it were absolutely more general than it as well, it would harm the mu'allil. Because (inna) matters, since the pronoun of the matter (damir al-sha'n) may be omitted even if weak, except with inna when it is lightened: By invalidating it, the opposite of the prevented thing is invalidated. And if its opposite is invalidated, then its essence is established. And its exposition: Invalidating the equal support establishes the prevented thing because its invalidation invalidates the opposite of the prevented thing, and everything that invalidates the opposite of the prevented thing establishes its essence, and everything that establishes its essence establishes the prevented thing. Thus invalidation establishes the prevented thing indirectly, due to the impossibility of the two contradictories being false. And it is a proof that says: and it establishes its essence, i.e., if it were not established just as its opposite is not established, then the two contradictories would be false, and the consequent is false. So his statement 'the impossibility etc.' is an indication of denying the consequent. And the explanation of this falsity, or the clarification that the support is equal to the objection, or the clarification that invalidating the equal support is a proof of the prevented thing: (that the meaning of equality of the support to the objection) (Know that its meaning is the persistence of the objection when its support persists and its falling when it falls. And the explanation is only specified to these two because they are limited to them in the opinion of the objector. And its being more specific (i.e., the support) than it (i.e., the objection) is its equality to the opposite of the prevented thing and its being more specific than it, because the well-known view is that the relation is only in terms of verification and in reference to the opposite. As for the less well-known, it is in reference to the obscurity of the prevented thing for the objector, because the core of the prevented thing is its obscurity for him, such that if the prevented thing were clear to him and he were ignorant of its composite, his objection would be obstinacy. So from this it is known that the relation between the opposite and obscurity for him is generality in one respect. Then this support must also be equal or more specific in the opinion of the objector, even if it is otherwise in reality. Example of the equal support: when the sa'il objects to a premise and bases it on his saying: How? It is not clear to me. Example of absolutely more general: How? It is not established to me by proof. Example of more specific: How? I am uncertain about it. Example of more general in one respect: How? I am not convinced. And that proof is of two types: One is proof by essence, meaning without the intermediate step of invalidating the support, and it is (mentioning a proof) really or effectively, with clarification that removes the objection, and explaining the doctrine similarly (yields) the prevented thing. And the other is proof by mediation (invalidating the support that is equal) in reality (to the objection) or absolutely more general than it only—he did not mention it due to its rarity or because the sa'il does not acknowledge its being more general, and if he did acknowledge it, he would invalidate his own basis, and so its invalidation would be proof by essence, not by mediation. And the prevented thing is only established by invalidating the equal support because (inna) it (i.e., the matter) by its invalidation invalidates the opposite of the prevented thing, so then its essence is established due to the impossibility of the two contradictories being false. And the explanation of this falsity (i.e., the falsity of the opposite of the prevented thing by that invalidation) is that the meaning of equality of the support to the objection and its being more specific than it is its equality to the opposite of the prevented thing and its being more specific than it, because the well-known view is that the relations in the support are considered in reference to the opposite of the prevented thing. That is, their statement 'this support is equal to the objection and more specific than it' is a metaphor in the attribution, and the intended meaning is equality to the opposite of the prevented thing or more specific than it, with correlation (mulabasa) between the objection and that equality and specificity. For the objection is like a place for what he said in the exposition, so know it. And it is not far to say that what is meant by objection here is the opposite of the prevented thing due to the concomitance (luzum) between them. So that.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the duties of the mu'allil (the one providing the argument) when the sa'il (the questioner) objects. It begins by stating that the mu'allil is obliged—customarily and even religiously—to prove what was objected to, whether the objection is to an unproven claim or to a premise of the proof. The proof (ithbat) can take several forms: presenting evidence, invalidating the support (sanad) that is equal to the opposite of the prevented thing, clarifying the meaning of parts, explaining the underlying doctrine, etc. The author then argues why this proof is obligatory: it is demanded by the objector, and every such demand is obligatory on the mu'allil. Next, the passage distinguishes two types of proof: direct (bi al-dhat) and indirect (bi al-wasita). Direct proof involves adducing evidence without the intermediate step of invalidating the support. Indirect proof works by invalidating the support that is equal to the objection, which in turn invalidates the opposite of the prevented thing, and due to the law of non-contradiction (impossibility of both contradictories being false), the prevented thing itself is established. The passage elaborates on the conditions of the support: it must be equal (musawi) or more specific (akhass) than the objection in the opinion of the objector. Examples are given: if the sa'il objects saying 'How? It is not clear to me' (equal support), or 'It is not established by proof' (more general), or 'I am uncertain' (more specific), or 'I am not convinced' (more general in one respect). The author also notes that if the sa'il acknowledges the support is more general, the indirect proof becomes direct. The passage concludes with a metaphorical interpretation, stating that 'equality to the objection' is a metaphor for equality to the opposite of the prevented thing, due to the relationship (luzum) between the objection and its opposite. This complex analysis aims to clarify the mechanics of rebuttal in dialectical argumentation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mu'allil obliged to do when the sa'il objects?
A) Ignore the objection and continue the argument
B) Prove what was objected to
C) Counter-attack with a new objection
D) Ask the sa'il to provide proof

Key Takeaway
The mu'allil is obligated to prove the objected claim; direct proof accomplishes this without attacking the sa'il's support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: In a theological debate, a mutakallim argues that 'the world is temporally originated (hadith)'. The opponent objects, saying 'Your premise that everything temporally originated has a cause is not proven.' How should the mutakallim use direct proof to respond? Provide a short answer referencing the forms listed in the passage.

Dialectic -- Jadal
advanced
10 min
Indirect Proof and the Condition of the Support
Explains indirect proof and the conditions of the support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how indirect proof (ithbat bi al-wasita) works by invalidating the support (sanad) and determine whether a given support is equal or more specific relative to the objection.

Core Idea
Indirect proof establishes the objected claim by invalidating its opposite, relying on the impossibility of both contradictories being false.

When direct proof is not used, the mu'allil may resort to indirect proof by attacking the support (sanad) that the sa'il gives for his objection. This lesson unpacks the mechanics of indirect proof and the crucial condition that the support must be equal or more specific than the objection in the objector's view.

Guided Path
1
Understand the indirect method
The passage describes indirect proof as invalidating the support that is equal to the objection. This invalidation causes the opposite (naqid) of the prevented thing to become false, and due to the impossibility of both contradictories being false, the prevented thing itself is established.
This relies on the law of non-contradiction.
2
Identify the necessary condition for the support
The support must be equal (musawi) or more specific (akhass) than the objection in the opinion of the objector. If it is more general, the method fails or becomes direct.
The passage gives examples of each.
3
Examine the examples
An equal support example: the sa'il says 'How? It is not clear to me.' A more specific support: 'I am uncertain.' A more general: 'It is not established by proof.' A more general in one respect: 'I am not convinced.'
The mu'allil must invalidate the support to proceed.
4
Recognize the logical foundation
The passage invokes 'istihalat irtifa' al-naqidayn' (impossibility of both contradictories being false). If the opposite of the prevented thing is invalidated, the prevented thing must be true.
This is a fundamental logical principle.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the duties of the mu'allil (the one providing the argument) when the sa'il (the questioner) objects. It begins by stating that the mu'allil is obliged—customarily and even religiously—to prove what was objected to, whether the objection is to an unproven claim or to a premise of the proof. The proof (ithbat) can take several forms: presenting evidence, invalidating the support (sanad) that is equal to the opposite of the prevented thing, clarifying the meaning of parts, explaining the underlying doctrine, etc. The author then argues why this proof is obligatory: it is demanded by the objector, and every such demand is obligatory on the mu'allil. Next, the passage distinguishes two types of proof: direct (bi al-dhat) and indirect (bi al-wasita). Direct proof involves adducing evidence without the intermediate step of invalidating the support. Indirect proof works by invalidating the support that is equal to the objection, which in turn invalidates the opposite of the prevented thing, and due to the law of non-contradiction (impossibility of both contradictories being false), the prevented thing itself is established. The passage elaborates on the conditions of the support: it must be equal (musawi) or more specific (akhass) than the objection in the opinion of the objector. Examples are given: if the sa'il objects saying 'How? It is not clear to me' (equal support), or 'It is not established by proof' (more general), or 'I am uncertain' (more specific), or 'I am not convinced' (more general in one respect). The author also notes that if the sa'il acknowledges the support is more general, the indirect proof becomes direct. The passage concludes with a metaphorical interpretation, stating that 'equality to the objection' is a metaphor for equality to the opposite of the prevented thing, due to the relationship (luzum) between the objection and its opposite. This complex analysis aims to clarify the mechanics of rebuttal in dialectical argumentation.

Key Terms
المعلل
السائل
الإثبات بالواسطة
السند
الممنوع
النقيض

A) The law of excluded middle
B) The impossibility of both contradictories being false (istihalat irtifa' al-naqidayn)
C) The principle of sufficient reason
D) The law of non-contradiction only applies to necessary truths

Two Types of Proof and the Meaning of Support Equality
Author defines direct and indirect proof and explains how indirect proof refutes a support to establish the denied claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish direct from indirect proof, identify the logical relationship between a support (sanad) and a denial (man'), and evaluate whether a given support is equal, more specific, or more general relative to the denial in a dialectical context.

Core Idea
Indirect proof refutes the opponent's support to establish the denied claim by necessity of contradictories.

In dialectical debate, when the opponent denies a claim, the refuter must either provide a direct proof or undermine the opponent's support for his denial. This passage from the Waladiyya explains the two types of proof and the crucial concept of musawa (equality) between the support and the denial. Understanding this distinction allows a debater to choose the right strategy and assess whether a refutation is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of proof
Find where the author divides proof into direct (bi al-dhat) and indirect (bi al-wasita). Note that direct proof provides a proof without refuting a support, while indirect proof first refutes the opponent's support.
The term 'ithbat' is used for proof in this dialectical context, not for categorical demonstration.
2
Understand the mechanism of indirect proof
Trace the logical chain: refuting the support (sanad) refutes the contradictory of the denied claim (naqid al-mamnu'), and since two contradictories cannot both be false, the denied claim itself is established. This is the principle of istihalat irtifa' al-naqidayn.
The support is provided by the opponent as the basis for his denial.
3
Grasp the meaning of equality (musawa)
Equality means the denial persists as long as its support persists and falls when the support falls. The support must be equal or more specific (akhass) relative to the denial in the opponent's view.
The relation is considered from the opponent's perspective, even if not true in reality.
4
Classify examples of support types
Analyze the four examples given: equal support ('It is not clear to me'), absolutely more general ('It is not proven to me'), more specific ('I am hesitant'), and more general in respect ('I have not decided'). Understand how each relates to the denial and the contradictory.
The more general in respect (a'am min wajh) shares part of its meaning with the contradictory and part with hiddenness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك الاثبات نوعان: أحدهما إثبات بالذات: أى بلا واسطة إبطال السند، وهو ذكر دليل حقيقة أو حكما في التحرير وبيان المذهب ينتج عين الممنوع أو ما يساويه أو الأخص منه مطلقا إذا التقريب موجود في كل منها. والآخر إثبات بالواسطة، وهو إبطال السند المساوي للمنع أى المساوي لنقيض الممنوع. ويحتمل أن يكون نسبة المساواة إلى المنع مجازا عقليا، وكذا إبطال السند الأعم مطلقا منه في نفس الأمر الذي هو أعم من وجه من عين الممنوع لأنه لو كان أعم مطلقا من عينه أيضا لكان مضرا للمعلل. لأن بابطاله يبطل نقيض الممنوع وإذا بطل نقيضه فيثبت عينه. وتقريره إبطال السند المساوي يثبت الممنوع لأن إبطاله يبطل نقيض الممنوع، وكل ما يبطل نقيض الممنوع يثبت عينه وكل ما يثبت عينه يثبت الممنوع فالابطال يثبت الممنوع بالواسطة لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين. وبيان هذا البطلان أن معنى مساواة السند للمنع بقاء المنع عند بقاء سنده وسقوطه عند سقوطه. وإنما خص البيان بهما لانحصاره فيهما في زعم المانع. وأخصيته منه مساواته لنقيض الممنوع وأخصيته منه إذ المشهور أن النسب إنما هو باعتبار التحقق وبالقياس إلى النقيض. وأما في غير المشهور فبالقياس إلى خفاء الممنوع عند المانع لأن مدار الممنوع خفاؤه عنده حتى لو كان الممنوع واضحا عنده ولو يجهل مركب لكان منعه مكابرة. فعلم من هذا النسبة بين النقيض وبين الخفاء عنده عموم من وجه، ثم إن هذا السند أيضا يجب أن يكون مساويا أو أخص بزعم المانع وإن كان غيرهما في الواقع. مثال السند المساوي كما إذا منع السائل مقدمة واستند بقوله كيف وهي غير واضحة عندي، ومثال الأعم مطلقا كيف وهى غير ثابتة عندي ببرهان، ومثال الأخص كيف وأنا متردد فيها، ومثال الأعم من وجه كيف ولم أجزم.
And that proof is of two types: one is direct proof, meaning without intermediary refutation of the support, and it is the mention of a proof in reality or according to ruling in the [reformulation] and clarification of the doctrine, producing the identical [matter] of the denied claim or what is equal to it or more specific than it absolutely if the approximation is present in each. The other is indirect proof, which is refutation of the support that is equal to the denial, i.e., equal to the contradictory of the denied claim. It is possible that the relation of equality to the denial is a figurative mental relation, and similarly the refutation of the support that is absolutely more general than it in reality, which is more general in respect than the identical [matter] of the denied claim, because if it were absolutely more general than its identical [matter] as well, it would be harmful to the [one providing the] cause. Because by its refutation, the contradictory of the denied claim is refuted, and when its contradictory is refuted, its identical [matter] is established. Its explanation: refuting the equal support establishes the denied claim because its refutation refutes the contradictory of the denied claim, and everything that refutes the contradictory of the denied claim establishes its identical [matter], and everything that establishes its identical [matter] establishes the denied claim. So the refutation establishes the denied claim indirectly, due to the impossibility of the elevation of two contradictories. The clarification of this refutation is that the meaning of the equality of the support to the denial is the persistence of the denial when its support persists and its fall when its support falls. And he restricted the explanation to these two [cases] because they are limited to them in the opponent's view. And its being more specific [than the denial] is its equality to the contradictory of the denied claim and its being more specific than it, since it is well-known that the relations are considered with respect to actual existence and relative to the contradictory. As for [what is] not well-known, it is considered relative to the hiddenness of the denied claim from the opponent, because the basis of the denied claim is its hiddenness from him; so if the denied claim were clear to him, even if he were ignorant of a composite, his denial would be obstinate. From this, it is known that the relation between the contradictory and the hiddenness from him is generality in respect. Then, this support must also be equal or more specific in the opponent's view, even if it is otherwise in reality. An example of the equal support: when the questioner denies a premise and supports his denial by saying 'How so? It is not clear to me.' Example of the absolutely more general: 'How so? It is not established to me by proof.' Example of the more specific: 'How so? I am hesitant about it.' Example of the more general in respect: 'How so? I have not decided.'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines two types of proof (ithbat) used in dialectical debate (jadal/munazara). The first is direct proof (ithbat bi al-dhat), which directly provides a proof that produces the matter itself (ayn al-mamnu') or what is equal or more specific. The second is indirect proof (ithbat bi al-wasita), which refutes the support (sanad) that the opponent relies on for his denial. The author explains that refuting a support that is equal to the denial (i.e., equal to the contradictory of the denied claim) indirectly establishes the denied claim. The reasoning: refuting the support refutes the contradictory of the denied claim (naqid al-mamnu'), and refuting the contradictory necessarily establishes the original claim because the two contradictories cannot both be false. This is the principle of istihalat irtifa' al-naqidayn (impossibility of the elevation of two contradictories). The author then clarifies the meaning of 'equality of the support to the denial' (musawat al-sand li al-man'): the denial persists as long as its support persists, and falls when its support falls. Similarly, 'more specific' (akhass) means the support is equal or more specific relative to the contradictory of the denied claim. The examples given are typical dialectical responses: e.g., 'How so? It is not clear to me' (equal support), 'How so? It is not proven to me' (absolutely more general), 'How so? I am hesitant' (more specific), 'How so? I have not decided' (more general in respect).

Key Terms
إثبات (ithbat): The proof or demonstration of a claim. Here distinguished into direct (without refuting a support) and indirect (by refuting the opponent's support).
سند (sanad): The support or reason given by the opponent to back up his denial (man'). It is what the refuter seeks to refute in indirect proof.
منع (man'): The denial by the opponent of a premise or claim. The refuter aims to remove this denial either directly or indirectly.
نقيض (naqid): The contradictory of a proposition. Here, the contradictory of the denied claim (naqid al-mamnu') is used in the reasoning: refuting the support refutes the contradictory, thereby establishing the original.
مساواة (musawa): Equality in the sense that the support and the denial (or its contradictory) are such that the persistence of one entails the persistence of the other, and their fall entails each other's fall.
أخص (akhass): More specific; meaning the support is narrower in scope than the contradictory of the denied claim, or than the denial itself, in the opponent's view.
أعم (a'am): More general; the support is broader in scope than the contradictory or the denial. This can be absolutely general (a'am mutlaq) or general in respect (a'am min wajh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In indirect proof (ithbat bi al-wasita), what is the immediate result of refuting the support (sanad)?
A) The denied claim (al-mamnu') is directly established.
B) The contradictory of the denied claim (naqid al-mamnu') is refuted.
C) The opponent's entire argument is destroyed.
D) The matter becomes clear to both parties.

Key Takeaway
Indirect proof refutes the opponent's support to remove his denial, leveraging the impossibility of both contradictories being false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(b) 'Your claim is not clear to me.' (c) 'I am uncertain about that premise.' (d) 'I do not accept that because it contradicts another text I hold.' The Five Types of Support by Rational Probability
Enumeration of five types of support by rational probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given support statement into one of the five categories based on its logical relation to the contradictory of the denied claim.

Core Idea
Five logical relations define a support's strength in debate: equal, more specific, more general, more general in respect, or distinct.

In dialectical debate, a 'support' (sanad) is a statement that strengthens a denial. The author classifies supports into five types based solely on rational probability (ihtimal aqli), without recourse to empirical induction. Each type is defined by its relation to the contradictory (naqid) of the denied claim (mamnu').

Guided Path
1
Identify the denied claim and its contradictory
In a debate, the opponent denies a claim. For example, if the claim 'the world is created' is denied, the denied claim is 'the world is not created'. Its contradictory is 'the world is created'.
2
Understand the five logical relations
The author lists five possible relations: (1) equal to the contradictory, (2) absolutely more specific than the contradictory, (3) absolutely more general than the denied claim itself, (4) more general in respect than the contradictory, (5) distinct (completely different) from the contradictory.
Note that type 3 references the denied claim, not its contradictory.
3
Apply each type to an example
Take the denied claim 'it is not a human' (contradictory: 'it is a human'). A support 'it is an animal' is more general absolutely than 'it is a human'? Actually, 'it is an animal' is more general than 'it is a human', but it is related to the contradictory? The text says type 3 is more general than the denied claim. So 'it is an animal' is more general than 'it is not a human'? That might be different. Better to use the example from the text: 'We do not concede that it is not a human; why not allow that it is a human?' Here 'it is a human' is the support equal to the contradictory. For more specific, 'it is a rational animal' is more specific than 'it is a human'. For more general in respect, 'it is living' is more general than 'it is a human' in some respect.
Ensure you compare the support to the proper term: for types 1,2,4,5 compare to the contradictory; for type 3 compare to the denied claim.
4
Recognize the basis in rational probability
All five types are considered possible as supports because they are logically consistent, not because they are empirically observed. Induction will later reduce them.
This classification is theoretical, not based on actual debate practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلافها والسند أى ما يطلق عليه لفظ السند ملتبسا بالاحتمال العقلى من غير أن يرجع إلى الاستقراء سواء كان له وجود في المناظرات أولا خمسة أقسام منحصرا فيها. الأول السند المساوي لنقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والثاني السند الأخص مطلقا من نقيض، والثالث السند الأعم مطلقا من الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والرابع الأعم من وجه من نقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والخامس السند المباين لنقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر. قال في التقرير: وأما السند بالاستقراء فأربعة أقسام لأن السند المباين لم يوجد في كلام المناظرين. وأما في زعم السائل فائنان. قال في الحاشية: إن قلت مساواة السند لنقيض الممنوع تشعر بمغايرته لنقيض الممنوع إذ لا يقال للشيء إنه مساو لعينه إذ المساواة تقتضي التعدد فالسند الذي هو عين نقيض الممنوع خارج عن الأقسام الخمسة كقولك: لا نسلم أنه ليس بانسان لم لا يجوز أن يكون إنسانا. قلت لم يذكر في كتب هذا الفن كون السند عين نقيض الممنوع، فالظاهر أن ذكر نقيض الممنوع بعد المنع ليس بسند في عرف هذا الفن، بل هو تصوير للمنع. تأمل انتهى. وفيه نظر لأن المراد من النقيض هنا النقيض المشهور وهو النقيض المجازى كما يشهد به المثال وهو داخل في المساوي للنقيض الحقيقى وهو المراد هاهنا كما عرفته، ويؤيد ما قلنا تعريفهم السند بما يقوى المنع في زعم المانع؛ وقد اعترف بكون النقيض المشهور سندا فيما سبق من كلام صاحب المواقف.
Contrary to that, and the support (sanad), i.e., what the word 'support' is applied to, clothed in rational probability, without recourse to induction, whether it exists in debates or not, is five types, limited to them. The first: the support equal to the contradictory of the denied claim in reality. The second: the support absolutely more specific than the contradictory. The third: the support absolutely more general than the denied claim in reality. The fourth: the support more general in respect than the contradictory of the denied claim in reality. The fifth: the support that is distinct (mubayin) from the contradictory of the denied claim in reality. He said in al-Taqrir: As for the support by induction, it is four types, because the distinct support is not found in the speech of debaters. And as for in the claim of the questioner, it is two? He said in al-Hashiya: If you say: The equality of the support to the contradictory of the denied claim suggests its being other than the contradictory of the denied claim, because one does not say of a thing that it is equal to itself, since equality requires multiplicity. So the support which is exactly the contradictory of the denied claim is excluded from the five types, as in your statement: 'We do not concede that it is not a human; why not allow that it is a human?' I say: It is not mentioned in the books of this art that the support is exactly the contradictory of the denied claim. So evidently, the mention of the contradictory of the denied claim after the denial is not a support in the usage of this art; rather, it is a representation of the denial. Reflect. End. There is a consideration, because what is meant by 'contradictory' here is the well-known contradictory, i.e., the figurative contradictory, as the example bears witness, and it is included in the equal to the real contradictory, which is what is intended here, as you have understood. And what we say is supported by their definition of the support as that which strengthens the denial in the opponent's view; and he has acknowledged that the well-known contradictory is a support in what was previously said by the author of al-Mawaqif.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the support (sanad) in dialectical exchange. The author first enumerates five types of support according to rational probability (al-ihtimal al-aqli), i.e., logical possibilities without empirical observation. These are defined by their relation to the contradictory (naqid) of the denied claim (al-mamnu'): (1) equal (musawi) to the contradictory, (2) absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq) than the contradictory, (3) absolutely more general (a'am mutlaq) than the denied claim itself, (4) more general in respect (a'am min wajh) than the contradictory, and (5) distinct (mubayin) from the contradictory. The author then cites al-Taqrir, stating that by induction (istiqra'), only four types are actually found in the speech of debaters; the distinct (mubayin) is not attested. Then there is an objection and answer from al-Hashiya: if a support is equal to the contradictory, that implies duality, so a support that is exactly the contradictory itself would not be included. The answer is that in the technical usage, the contradictory after a denial is not considered a support but a mere representation of the denial. However, the passage ends with a critical note suggesting that the intended 'contradictory' here is the figurative contradictory (al-naqid al-majazi), which is included in the equal category, and that this is consistent with the definition of support as that which strengthens the denial.

The Reduction to Four Types by Induction
Reduction from five to four types by induction, excluding the distinct support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why induction reduces the five rational types of support to four and identify which type is excluded.

Core Idea
Induction shows that only four types of support are used in debates; the distinct support never appears.

While rational probability admits five types of support, an empirical survey (istiqra') of actual debate practice reveals that the distinct (mubayin) support is never employed. This lesson explores this reduction and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Recall the five types from the previous lesson
The five types are based on rational probability: equal, absolutely more specific, absolutely more general, more general in respect, and distinct.
2
Read the induction statement from al-Taqrir
Al-Taqrir states: 'As for the support by induction, it is four types, because the distinct support is not found in the speech of debaters.' This means that when we look at actual debates, we never see a support that is completely unrelated to the contradictory.
Induction here refers to empirical observation of dialectical practice.
3
Understand why the distinct type is absent
A support that is distinct (mubayin) from the contradictory offers no logical strengthening to the denial. For example, if the denied claim is 'it is not a human', a completely unrelated statement like 'the sky is blue' does not support the denial. Therefore, it is effectively useless.
Rational probability still allows it, but practical usage excludes it.
4
Recognize the practical implication
Debaters only use supports that have a logical relation to the contradictory. This reduction shows that the art of debate is grounded in effective reasoning, not just logical possibility.
This highlights the pragmatic dimension of munazara.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلافها والسند أى ما يطلق عليه لفظ السند ملتبسا بالاحتمال العقلى من غير أن يرجع إلى الاستقراء سواء كان له وجود في المناظرات أولا خمسة أقسام منحصرا فيها. الأول السند المساوي لنقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والثاني السند الأخص مطلقا من نقيض، والثالث السند الأعم مطلقا من الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والرابع الأعم من وجه من نقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والخامس السند المباين لنقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر. قال في التقرير: وأما السند بالاستقراء فأربعة أقسام لأن السند المباين لم يوجد في كلام المناظرين. وأما في زعم السائل فائنان. قال في الحاشية: إن قلت مساواة السند لنقيض الممنوع تشعر بمغايرته لنقيض الممنوع إذ لا يقال للشيء إنه مساو لعينه إذ المساواة تقتضي التعدد فالسند الذي هو عين نقيض الممنوع خارج عن الأقسام الخمسة كقولك: لا نسلم أنه ليس بانسان لم لا يجوز أن يكون إنسانا. قلت لم يذكر في كتب هذا الفن كون السند عين نقيض الممنوع، فالظاهر أن ذكر نقيض الممنوع بعد المنع ليس بسند في عرف هذا الفن، بل هو تصوير للمنع. تأمل انتهى. وفيه نظر لأن المراد من النقيض هنا النقيض المشهور وهو النقيض المجازى كما يشهد به المثال وهو داخل في المساوي للنقيض الحقيقى وهو المراد هاهنا كما عرفته، ويؤيد ما قلنا تعريفهم السند بما يقوى المنع في زعم المانع؛ وقد اعترف بكون النقيض المشهور سندا فيما سبق من كلام صاحب المواقف.
Contrary to that, and the support (sanad), i.e., what the word 'support' is applied to, clothed in rational probability, without recourse to induction, whether it exists in debates or not, is five types, limited to them. The first: the support equal to the contradictory of the denied claim in reality. The second: the support absolutely more specific than the contradictory. The third: the support absolutely more general than the denied claim in reality. The fourth: the support more general in respect than the contradictory of the denied claim in reality. The fifth: the support that is distinct (mubayin) from the contradictory of the denied claim in reality. He said in al-Taqrir: As for the support by induction, it is four types, because the distinct support is not found in the speech of debaters. And as for in the claim of the questioner, it is two? He said in al-Hashiya: If you say: The equality of the support to the contradictory of the denied claim suggests its being other than the contradictory of the denied claim, because one does not say of a thing that it is equal to itself, since equality requires multiplicity. So the support which is exactly the contradictory of the denied claim is excluded from the five types, as in your statement: 'We do not concede that it is not a human; why not allow that it is a human?' I say: It is not mentioned in the books of this art that the support is exactly the contradictory of the denied claim. So evidently, the mention of the contradictory of the denied claim after the denial is not a support in the usage of this art; rather, it is a representation of the denial. Reflect. End. There is a consideration, because what is meant by 'contradictory' here is the well-known contradictory, i.e., the figurative contradictory, as the example bears witness, and it is included in the equal to the real contradictory, which is what is intended here, as you have understood. And what we say is supported by their definition of the support as that which strengthens the denial in the opponent's view; and he has acknowledged that the well-known contradictory is a support in what was previously said by the author of al-Mawaqif.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the support (sanad) in dialectical exchange. The author first enumerates five types of support according to rational probability (al-ihtimal al-aqli), i.e., logical possibilities without empirical observation. These are defined by their relation to the contradictory (naqid) of the denied claim (al-mamnu'): (1) equal (musawi) to the contradictory, (2) absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq) than the contradictory, (3) absolutely more general (a'am mutlaq) than the denied claim itself, (4) more general in respect (a'am min wajh) than the contradictory, and (5) distinct (mubayin) from the contradictory. The author then cites al-Taqrir, stating that by induction (istiqra'), only four types are actually found in the speech of debaters; the distinct (mubayin) is not attested. Then there is an objection and answer from al-Hashiya: if a support is equal to the contradictory, that implies duality, so a support that is exactly the contradictory itself would not be included. The answer is that in the technical usage, the contradictory after a denial is not considered a support but a mere representation of the denial. However, the passage ends with a critical note suggesting that the intended 'contradictory' here is the figurative contradictory (al-naqid al-majazi), which is included in the equal category, and that this is consistent with the definition of support as that which strengthens the denial.

Key Terms
استقراء
مباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Taqrir, how many types of support are found by induction?
A) Five
B) Four
C) Three
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Induction reduces the five rational types to four because the distinct support is never used in actual debates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a debate on a fiqh issue (e.g., whether nabidh is intoxicating). The opponent denies it is intoxicating. Pick a support that would be distinct (mubayin) from the contradictory and explain why it would be ineffective in actual debate.

The Contradictory as Support: Real vs Figurative
Objection and resolution regarding whether the contradictory itself can be a support, distinguishing real and figurative contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the real contradictory (naqid haqiqi) and the figurative contradictory (naqid majazi), and determine when a statement counts as a support versus a mere representation of the denial.

Core Idea
The real contradictory itself is not a support but a restatement of the denial; only the figurative contradictory qualifies as a support equal to the real contradictory.

A subtle objection arises: if a support equals the contradictory, does that include the case where the support is exactly the contradictory statement? The commentators clarify that in technical usage, the contradictory after a denial is not a support but a representation. However, what is called 'contradictory' in the classification is the figurative contradictory, which is indeed a support.

Guided Path
1
State the objection from al-Hashiya
The objection notes that equality requires two distinct things. If the support is exactly the contradictory of the denied claim (e.g., saying 'it is a human' after denying 'it is not a human'), then it is not a support but a repetition.
The example given: 'We do not concede that it is not a human; why not allow that it is a human?'
2
Understand the main answer
The answer in al-Hashiya is that in the art of debate, the contradictory after a denial is not considered a support; it is merely a representation of the denial (taswir li-l-man'). So the classification of five types does not include that case.
3
Explore the critical note
The author then says 'There is a consideration' (fihi nazar). He points out that the term 'contradictory' in the classification refers to the figurative contradictory (al-naqid al-majazi), which is different from the real contradictory. The figurative contradictory, such as 'it is not clear' vs 'it is clear', is indeed a support equal to the real contradictory.
The real contradictory would be 'it is a human' vs 'it is not a human'. The figurative is a looser opposition used in debates.
4
Apply the distinction
Recognize that when a debater says 'We do not concede it is not a human; why not allow that it is a human?', the statement 'it is a human' is the real contradictory. If considered as a support, it would be excluded per the answer. But if the opponent says 'it is not clear', the support 'it is clear' is figurative contradictory and is a valid support.
This distinction resolves the objection and aligns with the definition of support as something that strengthens the denial.

Source Text

بخلافها والسند أى ما يطلق عليه لفظ السند ملتبسا بالاحتمال العقلى من غير أن يرجع إلى الاستقراء سواء كان له وجود في المناظرات أولا خمسة أقسام منحصرا فيها. الأول السند المساوي لنقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والثاني السند الأخص مطلقا من نقيض، والثالث السند الأعم مطلقا من الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والرابع الأعم من وجه من نقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر، والخامس السند المباين لنقيض الممنوع في نفس الأمر. قال في التقرير: وأما السند بالاستقراء فأربعة أقسام لأن السند المباين لم يوجد في كلام المناظرين. وأما في زعم السائل فائنان. قال في الحاشية: إن قلت مساواة السند لنقيض الممنوع تشعر بمغايرته لنقيض الممنوع إذ لا يقال للشيء إنه مساو لعينه إذ المساواة تقتضي التعدد فالسند الذي هو عين نقيض الممنوع خارج عن الأقسام الخمسة كقولك: لا نسلم أنه ليس بانسان لم لا يجوز أن يكون إنسانا. قلت لم يذكر في كتب هذا الفن كون السند عين نقيض الممنوع، فالظاهر أن ذكر نقيض الممنوع بعد المنع ليس بسند في عرف هذا الفن، بل هو تصوير للمنع. تأمل انتهى. وفيه نظر لأن المراد من النقيض هنا النقيض المشهور وهو النقيض المجازى كما يشهد به المثال وهو داخل في المساوي للنقيض الحقيقى وهو المراد هاهنا كما عرفته، ويؤيد ما قلنا تعريفهم السند بما يقوى المنع في زعم المانع؛ وقد اعترف بكون النقيض المشهور سندا فيما سبق من كلام صاحب المواقف.

I say: It is not mentioned in the books of this art that the support is exactly the contradictory of the denied claim.
